ii

Introduction
Topology is an important and interesting area of mathematics, the study of
which will not only introduce you to new concepts and theorems but also put
into context old ones like continuous functions. However, to say just this is to
understate the signiï¬cance of topology. It is so fundamental that its inï¬‚uence
is evident in almost every other branch of mathematics. This makes the study
of topology relevant to all who aspire to be mathematicians whether their
ï¬rst love is (or will be) algebra, analysis, category theory, chaos, continuum
mechanics, dynamics, geometry, industrial mathematics, mathematical biology,
mathematical
economics,
mathematical
ï¬nance,
mathematical
modelling,
mathematical
physics,
mathematics
of
communication,
number
theory,
numerical mathematics, operations research or statistics. Topological notions
like compactness, connectedness and denseness are as basic to mathematicians
of today as sets and functions were to those of last century.
Topology has several diï¬€erent branches â€” general topology (also known
as point-set topology), algebraic topology, diï¬€erential topology and topological
algebra â€” the ï¬rst, general topology, being the door to the study of the others.
We aim in this book to provide a thorough grounding in general topology.
Anyone who conscientiously studies about the ï¬rst ten chapters and solves at
least half of the exercises will certainly have such a grounding.
For the reader who has not previously studied an axiomatic branch of
mathematics such as abstract algebra, learning to write proofs will be a hurdle.
To assist you to learn how to write proofs, quite often in the early chapters, we
include an aside which does not form part of the proof but outlines the thought
process which led to the proof. Asides are indicated in the following manner:
In order to arrive at the proof, we went through this thought process,
which might well be called the â€œdiscoveryâ€ or â€œexperiment phaseâ€.
However, the reader will learn that while discovery or experimentation
is often essential, nothing can replace a formal proof.

Only by working through a good
number of exercises will you master this course.

concepts in the exercises; the concepts which we consider most important will
generally be introduced again in the text.
Harder exercises are indicated by an *.

Chapter 1
Topological Spaces
Introduction
Tennis, football, baseball and hockey may all be exciting games but to play
them you must ï¬rst learn (some of) the rules of the game. Mathematics is no
diï¬€erent. So we begin with the rules for topology.
This chapter opens with the deï¬nition of a topology and is then devoted
to some simple examples: ï¬nite topological spaces, discrete spaces, indiscrete
spaces, and spaces with the ï¬nite-closed topology.
Topology, like other branches of pure mathematics such as group theory, is
an axiomatic subject. We start with a set of axioms and we use these axioms
to prove propositions and theorems. It is extremely important to develop your
skill at writing proofs.
Why are proofs so important?
Suppose our task were to construct a
building.
We would start with the foundations.
In our case these are the
axioms or deï¬nitions â€“ everything else is built upon them.
Each theorem or
proposition represents a new level of knowledge and must be ï¬rmly anchored to
the previous level. We attach the new level to the previous one using a proof.
So the theorems and propositions are the new heights of knowledge we achieve,
while the proofs are essential as they are the mortar which attaches them to
the level below. Without proofs the structure would collapse.
So what is a mathematical proof?
A mathematical proof is a watertight
argument which begins with information you are given, proceeds by logical
argument, and ends with what you are asked to prove.
1

2
CHAPTER 1.
TOPOLOGICAL SPACES
You should begin a proof by writing down the information you are given
and then state what you are asked to prove. If the information you are given or
what you are required to prove contains technical terms, then you should write
down the deï¬nitions of those technical terms.
Every proof should consist of complete sentences. Each of these sentences
should be a consequence of (i) what has been stated previously or (ii) a theorem,
proposition or lemma that has already been proved.
In this book you will see many proofs, but note that mathematics is not a
spectator sport. It is a game for participants. The only way to learn to write
proofs is to try to write them yourself.
1.1
Topology
1.1.1
Deï¬nitions.
Let X be a non-empty set. A collection Ï„ of
subsets of X is said to be a topology on X if
(i) X and the empty set, Ã˜, belong to Ï„ ,
(ii) the union of any (ï¬nite or inï¬nite) number of sets in Ï„ belongs to Ï„ ,
and
(iii) the intersection of any two sets in Ï„ belongs to Ï„ .
The pair (X,Ï„ ) is called a topological space.
1.1.2
Example.
Let X = {a, b, c, d, e, f} and
Ï„ 1 = {X, Ã˜, {a}, {c, d}, {a, c, d}, {b, c, d, e, f}}.
Then Ï„ 1 is a topology on X as it satisï¬es conditions (i), (ii) and (iii) of
Deï¬nitions 1.1.1.
1.1.3
Example.
Let X = {a, b, c, d, e} and
Ï„ 2 = {X, Ã˜, {a}, {c, d}, {a, c, e}, {b, c, d}}.
Then Ï„ 2 is not a topology on X as the union
{c, d} âˆª{a, c, e} = {a, c, d, e}
of two members of Ï„ 2 does not belong to Ï„ 2 ; that is, Ï„ 2 does not satisfy
condition (ii) of Deï¬nitions 1.1.1.

1.1.6
Deï¬nitions.
Let X be any non-empty set and let Ï„ be the

set X. The topological space (X,Ï„ ) is called a discrete space.
We note that Ï„ in Deï¬nitions 1.1.6 does satisfy the conditions of Deï¬nitions
1.1.1 and so is indeed a topology.
Observe that the set X in Deï¬nitions 1.1.6 can be any non-empty set. So
there is an inï¬nite number of discrete spaces â€“ one for each set X.
1.1.7
Deï¬nitions.
Let X be any non-empty set and Ï„ = {X, Ã˜}.
Then Ï„
is called the indiscrete topology and (X,Ï„ ) is said to be an
indiscrete space.
Once again we have to check that Ï„ satisï¬es the conditions of Deï¬nitions
1.1.1 and so is indeed a topology.
We observe again that the set X in Deï¬nitions 1.1.7 can be any non-empty
set. So there is an inï¬nite number of indiscrete spaces â€“ one for each set X.

5
In the introductory comments on this chapter we observed that mathematics
is not a spectator sport.
You should be an active participant.
Of course
your participation includes doing some of the exercises. But more than this is
expected of you. You have to think about the material presented to you.
One of your tasks is to look at the results that we prove and to ask pertinent
questions. For example, we have just shown that if each of the singleton sets
{a}, {b} and {c} is in Ï„ and X = {a, b, c}, then Ï„ is the discrete topology.
You
should ask if this is but one example of a more general phenomenon; that is,
if (X,Ï„ ) is any topological space such that Ï„ contains every singleton set, is Ï„
necessarily the discrete topology?
The answer is â€œyesâ€, and this is proved in
Proposition 1.1.9.

6
CHAPTER 1.

1.1.9
Proposition.
If (X,Ï„ ) is a topological space such that, for
every x âˆˆX, the singleton set {x} is in Ï„ , then Ï„ is the discrete topology.
Proof.
This result is a generalization of Example 1.1.8. Thus you might expect
that the proof would be similar.
However, we cannot list all of the
subsets of X as we did in Example 1.1.8 because X may be an inï¬nite
set. Nevertheless we must prove that every subset of X is in Ï„ .
At this point you may be tempted to prove the result for some special
cases, for example taking X to consist of 4, 5 or even 100 elements.
But this approach is doomed to failure. Recall our opening comments
in this chapter where we described a mathematical proof as a watertight
argument. We cannot produce a watertight argument by considering
a few special cases, or even a very large number of special cases. The
watertight argument must cover all cases. So we must consider the
general case of an arbitrary non-empty set X. Somehow we must prove
that every subset of X is in Ï„ .
Looking again at the proof of Example 1.1.8 we see that the key
is that every subset of X is a union of singleton subsets of X and we
already know that all of the singleton subsets are in Ï„ .
This is also
true in the general case.
We begin the proof by recording the fact that every set is a union of its
singleton subsets. Let S be any subset of X. Then
S =

xâˆˆS
{x}.
Since we are given that each {x} is in Ï„ , Deï¬nitions 1.1.1 (ii) and the above
equation imply that S âˆˆÏ„ . As S is an arbitrary subset of X, we have that Ï„ is
the discrete topology.
That every set S is a union of its singleton subsets is a result which we shall
use from time to time throughout the book in many diï¬€erent contexts. Note
that it holds even when S = Ã˜ as then we form what is called an empty union
and get Ã˜ as the result.

1.2
Open Sets, Closed Sets, and Clopen Sets
Rather than continually refer to â€œmembers of Ï„ â€, we ï¬nd it more convenient
to give such sets a name.
We call them â€œopen setsâ€.
We shall also name
the complements of open sets.
They will be called â€œclosed setsâ€.
This
nomenclature is not ideal, but derives from the so-called â€œopen intervalsâ€ and
â€œclosed intervalsâ€ on the real number line. We shall have more to say about
this in Chapter 2.
1.2.1
Deï¬nition.
Let (X,Ï„ ) be any topological space. Then the
members of Ï„ are said to be open sets.
1.2.2
Proposition.
If (X,Ï„ ) is any topological space, then
(i) X and Ã˜ are open sets,
(ii) the union of any (ï¬nite or inï¬nite) number of open sets is an open set
and
(iii) the intersection of any ï¬nite number of open sets is an open set.
Proof.
Clearly (i) and (ii) are trivial consequences of Deï¬nition 1.2.1 and
Deï¬nitions 1.1.1 (i) and (ii). The condition (iii) follows from Deï¬nition 1.2.1
and Exercises 1.1 #4.
On reading Proposition 1.2.2, a question should have popped into your
mind: while any ï¬nite or inï¬nite union of open sets is open, we state only that
ï¬nite intersections of open sets are open.
Are inï¬nite intersections of open
sets always open? The next example shows that the answer is â€œnoâ€.

1.2.3
Example.
Let N be the set of all positive integers and let Ï„
consist of Ã˜ and each subset S of N such that the complement of S in N, N \ S,
is a ï¬nite set. It is easily veriï¬ed that Ï„ satisï¬es Deï¬nitions 1.1.1 and so is a

called the ï¬nite-closed topology.) For each natural number n, deï¬ne the set Sn

n=1

(1)
As the complement of {1} is neither N nor a ï¬nite set, {1} is not open. So (1)

You might well ask: how did you ï¬nd the example presented in Example
1.2.3? The answer is unglamorous! It was by trial and error.
If we tried, for example, a discrete topology, we would ï¬nd that each
intersection of open sets is indeed open.
The same is true of the indiscrete
topology. So what you need to do is some intelligent guesswork.
Remember that to prove that the intersection of open sets is not necessarily
open, you need to ï¬nd just one counterexample!
1.2.4
Deï¬nition.
Let (X,Ï„ ) be a topological space. A subset S of
X is said to be a closed set in (X,Ï„ ) if its complement in X, namely X \ S,
is open in (X,Ï„ ).
In Example 1.1.2, the closed sets are
Ã˜, X, {b, c, d, e, f}, {a, b, e, f}, {b, e, f} and {a}.
If (X,Ï„ ) is a discrete space, then it is obvious that every subset of X is a closed
set. However in an indiscrete space, (X,Ï„ ), the only closed sets are X and Ã˜.

1.2.5
Proposition.
If (X,Ï„ ) is any topological space, then
(i) Ã˜ and X are closed sets,
(ii) the intersection of any (ï¬nite or inï¬nite) number of closed sets is a
closed set and
(iii) the union of any ï¬nite number of closed sets is a closed set.
Proof.
(i) follows immediately from Proposition 1.2.2 (i) and Deï¬nition
1.2.4, as the complement of X is Ã˜ and the complement of Ã˜ is X.
To prove that (iii) is true, let S1, S2, . . . , Sn be closed sets. We are required
to prove that S1 âˆªS2 âˆªÂ· Â· Â· âˆªSn is a closed set. It suï¬ƒces to show, by Deï¬nition
1.2.4, that X \ (S1 âˆªS2 âˆªÂ· Â· Â· âˆªSn) is an open set.
As S1, S2, . . . , Sn are closed sets, their complements X \ S1, X \ S2, . . . , X \ Sn
are open sets. But
X \ (S1 âˆªS2 âˆªÂ· Â· Â· âˆªSn) = (X \ S1) âˆ©(X \ S2) âˆ©Â· Â· Â· âˆ©(X \ Sn).
(1)
As the right hand side of (1) is a ï¬nite intersection of open sets, it is an
open set. So the left hand side of (1) is an open set. Hence S1 âˆªS2 âˆªÂ· Â· Â· âˆªSn is
a closed set, as required. So (iii) is true.
The proof of (ii) is similar to that of (iii). [However, you should read the
warning in the proof of Example 1.3.9.]
Warning.
The names â€œopenâ€ and â€œclosedâ€ often lead newcomers to the
world of topology into error. Despite the names, some open sets are also closed
sets! Moreover, some sets are neither open sets nor closed sets! Indeed, if we
consider Example 1.1.2 we see that
(i) the set {a} is both open and closed;
(ii) the set {b, c} is neither open nor closed;
(iii) the set {c, d} is open but not closed;
(iv) the set {a, b, e, f} is closed but not open.
In a discrete space every set is both open and closed, while in an indiscrete
space (X,Ï„ ), all subsets of X except X and Ã˜ are neither open nor closed.

To remind you that sets can be both open and closed we introduce the
following deï¬nition.
1.2.6
Deï¬nition.
A subset S of a topological space (X,Ï„ ) is said
to be clopen if it is both open and closed in (X,Ï„ ).
In every topological space (X,Ï„ ) both X and Ã˜ are clopen1.
In a discrete space all subsets of X are clopen.
In an indiscrete space the only clopen subsets are X and Ã˜.

1.3
The Finite-Closed Topology
It is usual to deï¬ne a topology on a set by stating which sets are open. However,
sometimes it is more natural to describe the topology by saying which sets are
closed. The next deï¬nition provides one such example.
1.3.1
Deï¬nition.
Let X be any non-empty set. A topology Ï„ on
X is called the ï¬nite-closed topology or the coï¬nite topology if the closed
subsets of X are X and all ï¬nite subsets of X; that is, the open sets are Ã˜
and all subsets of X which have ï¬nite complements.
Once again it is necessary to check that Ï„ in Deï¬nition 1.3.1 is indeed a
topology; that is, that it satisï¬es each of the conditions of Deï¬nitions 1.1.1.
Note that Deï¬nition 1.3.1 does not say that every topology which has X
and the ï¬nite subsets of X closed is the ï¬nite-closed topology. These must be
the only closed sets. [Of course, in the discrete topology on any set X, the set
X and all ï¬nite subsets of X are indeed closed, but so too are all other subsets
of X.]
In the ï¬nite-closed topology all ï¬nite sets are closed. However, the following
example shows that inï¬nite subsets need not be open sets.

14
CHAPTER 1.
TOPOLOGICAL SPACES
1.3.2
Example.
If N is the set of all positive integers, then sets such as
{1}, {5, 6, 7}, {2, 4, 6, 8} are ï¬nite and hence closed in the ï¬nite-closed topology.
Thus their complements
{2, 3, 4, 5, . . .}, {1, 2, 3, 4, 8, 9, 10, . . .}, {1, 3, 5, 7, 9, 10, 11, . . .}
are open sets in the ï¬nite-closed topology.
On the other hand, the set of
even positive integers is not a closed set since it is not ï¬nite and hence its
complement, the set of odd positive integers, is not an open set in the ï¬nite-
closed topology.
So while all ï¬nite sets are closed, not all inï¬nite sets are open.
1.3.3
Example.
Let Ï„ be the ï¬nite-closed topology on a set X. If X
has at least 3 distinct clopen subsets, prove that X is a ï¬nite set.
Proof.
We are given that Ï„ is the ï¬nite-closed topology, and that there are at
least 3 distinct clopen subsets.
We are required to prove that X is a ï¬nite set.
Recall that Ï„ is the ï¬nite-closed topology means that the family of
all closed sets consists of X and all ï¬nite subsets of X. Recall also that
a set is clopen if and only if it is both closed and open.
Remember that in every topological space there are at least 2 clopen
sets, namely X and Ã˜.
(See the comment immediately following
Deï¬nition 1.2.6.) But we are told that in the space (X,Ï„ ) there are at
least 3 clopen subsets. This implies that there is a clopen subset other
than Ã˜ and X. So we shall have a careful look at this other clopen set!
As our space (X,Ï„ ) has 3 distinct clopen subsets, we know that there is
a clopen subset S of X such that S Ì¸= X and S Ì¸= Ã˜. As S is open in (X,Ï„ ),
Deï¬nition 1.2.4 implies that its complement X \ S is a closed set.
Thus S and X \ S are closed in the ï¬nite-closed topology Ï„ . Therefore S
and X \ S are both ï¬nite, since neither equals X. But X = S âˆª(X \ S) and so X
is the union of two ï¬nite sets. Thus X is a ï¬nite set, as required.

1.3.
FINITE-CLOSED TOPOLOGY
15
We now know three distinct topologies we can put on any inï¬nite set â€“
and there are many more. The three we know are the discrete topology, the
indiscrete topology, and the ï¬nite-closed topology. So we must be careful always
to specify the topology on a set.
For example, the set {n : n â‰¥10} is open in the ï¬nite-closed topology on
the set of natural numbers, but is not open in the indiscrete topology. The set
of odd natural numbers is open in the discrete topology on the set of natural
numbers, but is not open in the ï¬nite-closed topology.
We shall now record some deï¬nitions which you have probably met before.
1.3.4
Deï¬nitions.
Let f be a function from a set X into a set Y .
(i) The function f is said to be one-to-one or injective if f(x1) = f(x2)
implies x1 = x2, for x1, x2 âˆˆX;
(ii) The function f is said to be onto or surjective if for each y âˆˆY there
exists an x âˆˆX such that f(x) = y;
(iii) The function f is said to be bijective if it is both one-to-one and onto.
1.3.5
Deï¬nitions.
Let f be a function from a set X into a set Y .
The function f is said to have an inverse if there exists a function g of Y
into X such that g(f(x)) = x, for all x âˆˆX and f(g(y)) = y, for all y âˆˆY . The
function g is called an inverse function of f.
The proof of the following proposition is left as an exercise for you.
1.3.6
Proposition.
Let f be a function from a set X into a set Y .
(i) The function f has an inverse if and only if f is bijective.
(ii) Let g1 and g2 be functions from Y into X. If g1 and g2 are both inverse
functions of f, then g1 = g2; that is, g1(y) = g2(y), for all y âˆˆY .
(iii) Let g be a function from Y into X. Then g is an inverse function of f
if and only if f is an inverse function of g.

16
CHAPTER 1.
TOPOLOGICAL SPACES
Warning.
It is a very common error for students to think that a function is
one-to-one if â€œit maps one point to one pointâ€.
All functions map one point to one point. Indeed this is part of the deï¬nition
of a function.
A one-to-one function is a function that maps diï¬€erent points to diï¬€erent
points.
We now turn to a very important notion that you may not have met before.
1.3.7
Deï¬nition.
Let f be a function from a set X into a set Y .
If S is any subset of Y , then the set f âˆ’1(S) is deï¬ned by
f âˆ’1(S) = {x : x âˆˆX and f(x) âˆˆS}.
The subset f âˆ’1(S) of X is said to be the inverse image of S.
Note that an inverse function of f : X â†’Y exists if and only if f is bijective.
But the inverse image of any subset of Y exists even if f is neither one-to-one
nor onto. The next example demonstrates this.
1.3.8
Example.
Let f be the function from the set of integers, Z, into
itself given by f(z) = |z|, for each z âˆˆZ.
The function f is not one-to one, since f(1) = f(âˆ’1).
It is also not onto, since there is no z âˆˆZ, such that f(z) = âˆ’1.
So f is
certainly not bijective.
Hence, by Proposition 1.3.6 (i), f does not have an
inverse function. However inverse images certainly exist. For example,
f âˆ’1({1, 2, 3}) = {âˆ’1, âˆ’2, âˆ’3, 1, 2, 3}
f âˆ’1({âˆ’5, 3, 5, 7, 9}) = {âˆ’3, âˆ’5, âˆ’7, âˆ’9, 3, 5, 7, 9}.

1.4
Postscript
In this chapter we introduced the fundamental notion of a topological space.
As examples we saw various ï¬nite spaces, as well as discrete spaces, indiscrete
spaces and spaces with the ï¬nite-closed topology. None of these is a particularly
important example as far as applications are concerned. However, in Exercises
4.3 #8, it is noted that every inï¬nite topological space â€œcontainsâ€ an inï¬nite
topological space with one of the ï¬ve topologies: the indiscrete topology, the
discrete topology, the ï¬nite-closed topology, the initial segment topology, or the
ï¬nal segment topology of Exercises 1.1 #6. In the next chapter we describe
the very important Euclidean topology.
En route we met the terms â€œopen setâ€ and â€œclosed setâ€ and we were warned
that these names can be misleading. Sets can be both open and closed, neither
open nor closed, open but not closed, or closed but not open. It is important
to remember that we cannot prove that a set is open by proving that it is not
closed.
Other than the deï¬nitions of topology, topological space, open set, and
closed set the most signiï¬cant topic covered was that of writing proofs.
In the opening comments of this chapter we pointed out the importance
of learning to write proofs. In Example 1.1.8, Proposition 1.1.9, and Example
1.3.3 we have seen how to â€œthink throughâ€ a proof.
It is essential that you
develop your own skill at writing proofs. Good exercises to try for this purpose
include Exercises 1.1 #8, Exercises 1.2 #2,4, and Exercises 1.3 #1,4.
Some students are confused by the notion of topology as it involves â€œsets
of setsâ€. To check your understanding, do Exercises 1.1 #3.
The exercises included the notions of T0-space and T1-space which will be
formally introduced later. These are known as separation properties.
Finally we emphasize the importance of inverse images.
These are dealt
with in Example 1.3.9 and Exercises 1.3 #1.
Our deï¬nition of continuous
mapping will rely on inverse images.

Appendix 1: Inï¬nite Sets
Introduction
Once upon a time in a far-oï¬€land there were two hotels, the Hotel Finite (an
ordinary hotel with a ï¬nite number of rooms) and Hilbertâ€™s Hotel Inï¬nite (an
extra-ordinary hotel with an inï¬nite number of rooms numbered 1, 2, . . .n, . . . ).
One day a visitor arrived in town seeking a room. She went ï¬rst to the Hotel
Finite and was informed that all rooms were occupied and so she could not be
accommodated, but she was told that the other hotel, Hilbertâ€™s Hotel Inï¬nite,
can always ï¬nd an extra room. So she went to Hilbertâ€™s Hotel Inï¬nite and was
told that there too all rooms were occupied. However, the desk clerk said at
this hotel an extra guest can always be accommodated without evicting anyone.
He moved the guest from room 1 to room 2, the guest from room 2 to room
3, and so on. Room 1 then became vacant!
From this cute example we see that there is an intrinsic diï¬€erence between
inï¬nite sets and ï¬nite sets. The aim of this Appendix is to provide a gentle but
very brief introduction to the theory of Inï¬nite Sets. This is a fascinating topic
which, if you have not studied it before, will contain several surprises. We shall
learn that â€œinï¬nite sets were not created equalâ€ - some are bigger than others.
At ï¬rst pass it is not at all clear what this statement could possibly mean. We
will need to deï¬ne the term â€œbiggerâ€. Indeed we will need to deï¬ne what we
mean by â€œtwo sets are the same sizeâ€.

APPENDIX 1: INFINITE SETS
A1.1
Countable Sets
A1.1.1
Deï¬nitions.
Let A and B be sets. Then A is said to be
equipotent to B, denoted by A âˆ¼B, if there exists a function f : A â†’B
which is both one-to-one and onto (that is, f is a bijection or a one-to-one
correspondence).
A1.1.2
Proposition.
Let A, B, and C be sets.
(i) Then A âˆ¼A.
(ii) If A âˆ¼B then B âˆ¼A.
(iii) If A âˆ¼B and B âˆ¼C then A âˆ¼C.
Outline Proof.
(i) The identity function f on A, given by f(x) = x, for all x âˆˆA, is a one-to-one
correspondence between A and itself.
(ii) If f is a bijection of A onto B then it has an inverse function g from B to
A and g is also a one-to-one correspondence.
(iii) If f : A â†’B is a one-to-one correspondence and g : B â†’C is a one-to-one
correspondence, then their composition gf : A â†’C is also a one-to-one
correspondence.
Proposition A1.1.2 says that the relation â€œâˆ¼â€ is reï¬‚exive (i), symmetric (ii),
and transitive (iii); that is, â€œâˆ¼â€ is an equivalence relation.
A1.1.3
Proposition.
Let n, m âˆˆN. Then the sets {1, 2, . . ., n} and
{1, 2, . . ., m} are equipotent if and only if n = m.
Proof.
Exercise.
â–¡
Now we explicitly deï¬ne the terms â€œï¬nite setâ€ and â€œinï¬nite setâ€.

A1.1.4
Deï¬nitions.
Let S be a set.
(i) Then S is said to be ï¬nite if it is the empty set, Ã˜, or it is equipotent
to {1, 2, . . ., n}, for some n âˆˆN.
(ii) If S is not ï¬nite, then it is said to be inï¬nite.
(iii) If S âˆ¼{1, 2, . . ., n} then S is said to have cardinality n, which is denoted
by card S = n.
(iv) If S = Ã˜ then the cardinality is said to be 0, which is denoted by
card Ã˜ = 0.
The next step is to deï¬ne the â€œsmallestâ€ kind of inï¬nite set. Such sets will
be called countably inï¬nite.
At this stage we do not know that there is any
â€œbiggerâ€ kind of inï¬nite set â€“ indeed we do not even know what â€œbiggerâ€ would
mean in this context.
A1.1.5
Deï¬nitions.
Let S be a set.
(i) The set S is said to be countably inï¬nite (or denumerable) if it is
equipotent to N.
(ii) The set S is said to be countable if it is ï¬nite or countably inï¬nite.
(iii) If S is countably inï¬nite then it is said to have cardinality â„µ0, (or Ï‰),
denoted by card S = â„µ0 (or card S = Ï‰).
(iv) A set S is said to be uncountable if it is not countable.
A1.1.6
Remark.
We see that if the set S is countably inï¬nite, then
S = {s1, s2, . . . , sn, . . . } where f : N â†’S is a one-to-one correspondence and
sn = f(n), for all n âˆˆN.
So we can
list
the elements of S.
Of course if S
is ï¬nite and non-empty, we can also list its elements by S = {s1, s2, . . . , sn}. So we
can list the elements of any countable set. Conversely, if the elements of S can
be listed then S is countable as the listing deï¬nes a one-to-one correspondence
with N or {1, 2, . . ., n}.

countable.
Proof.
Since S is countable we can write it as a list S = {s1, s2, . . . } (a ï¬nite
list if S is ï¬nite, an inï¬nite one if S is countably inï¬nite).
Let t1 be the ï¬rst si in T (if T Ì¸= Ã˜). Let t2 be the second si in T (if T Ì¸= {t1}).
Let t3 be the third si in T (if T Ì¸= {t1, t2}), . . . .
This process comes to an end only if T = {t1, t2, . . . , tn} for some n, in which
case T is ï¬nite.
If the process does not come to an end we obtain a list
{t1, t2, . . . , tn, . . . } of members of T. This list contains every member of T, because
if si âˆˆT then we reach si no later than the ith step in the process; so si occurs
in the list. Hence T is countably inï¬nite.
So T is either ï¬nite or countably inï¬nite.
As an immediate consequence of Proposition 1.1.11 and Example 1.1.8 we
have the following result.

A1.1.26

k=1

A1.1.30
Deï¬nition.
Let S be any set. The set of all subsets of S
is said to be the power set of S and is denoted by P(S).
A1.1.31
Theorem.
(Georg Cantor)
For every set S, the power
set, P(S), is not equipotent to S; that is, P(S) Ì¸âˆ¼S.
Proof.
We have to prove that there is no one-to-one correspondence
between S and P(S). We shall prove more: that there is not even any surjective
function mapping S onto P(S).
Suppose that there exists a function f : S â†’P(S) which is onto. For each
x âˆˆS, f(x) âˆˆP(S), which is the same as saying that f(x) âŠ†S.
Let T = {x : x âˆˆS and x Ì¸âˆˆf(x)}. Then T âŠ†S; that is, T âˆˆP(S). So T = f(y)
for some y âˆˆS, since f maps S onto P(S). Now y âˆˆT or y Ì¸âˆˆT.
Case 1.
y âˆˆT â‡’y Ì¸âˆˆf(y)
(by the deï¬nition of T)
â‡’y Ì¸âˆˆT
(since f(y) = T).
So Case 1 is impossible.
Case 2.
y Ì¸âˆˆT â‡’y âˆˆf(y)
(by the deï¬nition of T)
â‡’y âˆˆT
(since f(y) = T).
So Case 2 is impossible.
As both cases are impossible, we have a contradiction. So our supposition
is false and there does not exist any function mapping S onto P(S). Thus P(S)
is not equipotent to S.

31
A1.1.32
Lemma.
If S is any set, then S is equipotent to a subset
of its power set, P(S).
Proof.
Deï¬ne the mapping f : S â†’P(S) by f(x) = {x}, for each x âˆˆS.
Clearly f is a one-to-one correspondence between the sets S and f(S). So S is
equipotent to the subset f(S) of P(S).
A1.1.33
Proposition.
If S is any inï¬nite set, then P(S) is an
uncountable set.
Proof.
As S is inï¬nite, the set P(S) is inï¬nite. By Theorem A1.1.30, P(S)
is not equipotent to S.
Suppose P(S) is countably inï¬nite. Then by Proposition A1.1.11, Lemma
1.1.31 and Proposition A1.1.10, S is countably inï¬nite.
So S and P(S) are
equipotent, which is a contradiction. Hence P(S) is uncountable.
Proposition A1.1.33 demonstrates the existence of uncountable sets. However
the sceptic may feel that the example is contrived. So we conclude this section
by observing that important and familiar sets are uncountable.

In the previous section we deï¬ned countably inï¬nite and uncountable and

Our exposition is based on that in Paul Halmosâ€™ book: â€œNaive Set Theoryâ€,

A1.2.1
Theorem.
(Cantor-SchrÂ¨oder-Bernstein)
Let S and
T be sets. If S is equipotent to a subset of T and T is equipotent to a subset
of S, then S is equipotent to T.
Proof.
Without loss of generality we can assume S and T are disjoint. Let
f : S â†’T and g : T â†’S be one-to-one maps. We are required to ï¬nd a bijection
of S onto T.
We say that an element s is a parent of an element f(s) and f(s) is a
descendant of s. Also t is a parent of g(t) and g(t) is a descendant of t. Each
s âˆˆS has an inï¬nite sequence of descendants: f(s), g(f(s)), f(g(f(s))), and so on.
We say that each term in such a sequence is an ancestor of all the terms that
follow it in the sequence.
Now let s âˆˆS. If we trace its ancestry back as far as possible one of three
things must happen:
(i) the list of ancestors is ï¬nite, and stops at an element of S which has no
ancestor;
(ii) the list of ancestors is ï¬nite, and stops at an element of T which has no
ancestor;
(iii) the list of ancestors is inï¬nite.
Let SS be the set of those elements in S which originate in S; that is, SS
is the set S \ g(T) plus all of its descendants in S. Let ST be the set of those
elements which originate in T; that is, ST is the set of descendants in S of
T \ f(S). Let Sâˆbe the set of all elements in S with no parentless ancestors.
Then S is the union of the three disjoint sets SS, ST and Sâˆ. Similarly T is the
disjoint union of the three similarly deï¬ned sets: TT, TS, and Tâˆ.
Clearly the restriction of f to SS is a bijection of SS onto TS.
Now let gâˆ’1 be the inverse function of the bijection g of T onto g(T). Clearly
the restriction of gâˆ’1 to ST is a bijection of ST onto TT.
Finally, the restriction of f to Sâˆis a bijection of Sâˆonto Tâˆ.
Deï¬ne h : S â†’T by
h(s) =
ï£±
ï£´
ï£²
ï£´
ï£³
f(s)
if s âˆˆSS
gâˆ’1(s)
if s âˆˆST
f(s)
if s âˆˆSâˆ.
Then h is a bijection of S onto T. So S is equipotent to T.

Our next task is to deï¬ne what we mean by â€œcardinal numberâ€.
A1.2.2
Deï¬nitions.
A collection, â„µ, of sets is said to be a cardinal
number if it satisï¬es the conditions:
(i) Let S and T be sets. If S and T are in â„µ, then S âˆ¼T;
(ii) Let A and B be sets. If A is in â„µand B âˆ¼A, then B is in â„µ.
If â„µis a cardinal number and A is a set in â„µ, then we write card A = â„µ.
Deï¬nitions A1.2.2 may, at ï¬rst sight, seem strange. A cardinal number is
deï¬ned as a collection of sets. So let us look at a couple of special cases:
If a set A has two elements we write card A = 2; the cardinal number 2 is
the collection of all sets eqipotent to the set {1, 2}, that is the collection of all
sets with 2 elements.
If a set S is countable inï¬nite, then we write card S = â„µ0; in this case the
cardinal number â„µ0 is the collection of all sets equipotent to N.
Let S and T be sets. Then S is equipotent to T if and only if card S = card T.
A1.2.3
Deï¬nitions.
The cardinality of R is denoted by c; that is,
card R = c. The cardinality of N is denoted by â„µ0.
The symbol c is used in Deï¬nitions A1.2.3 as we think of R as the
â€œcontinuumâ€.
We now deï¬ne an ordering of the cardinal numbers.
A1.2.4
Deï¬nitions.

cardinal m is said to be less than or equal to n, that is m â‰¤n, if there are
sets S and T such that card m = S, card T = n, and S is equipotent to a
subset of T. Further, the cardinal m is said to be strictly less than n, that
is m < n, if m â‰¤n and m Ì¸= n.

A1.3.11
Theorem.
2â„µ0 = c.
Proof.
Firstly observe that 2â„µ0 â‰¤â„µ0
â„µ0 = c, by Lemma A1.3.10.
So we

Each element x of [0, 1) has a binary representation
x = 0.x1x2 . . . xn . . . , with each xi equal to 0 or 1.
The binary representation is

1/4 = 0.0100 . . .0 Â· Â· Â· = 0.0011 . . .1 . . ..
Providing that in all such cases we choose the representation with a string

unique.
We deï¬ne the function f : [0, 1) â†’{0, 1}N which maps x âˆˆ[0, 1) to the

consider any x and y in [0, 1) with x Ì¸= y.
Then xm Ì¸= ym, for some m âˆˆN.
So f(x)(m) = xm Ì¸= ym = f(y)(m).
Hence the two functions f(x): N â†’{0, 1} and
f(y): N â†’{0, 1} are not equal. As x and y were arbitrary (unequal) elements of
[0, 1), it follows that f is indeed injective, as required.

In a movie there is usually a character about whom the plot revolves.
In the story of topology, the Euclidean topology on the set of real
numbers is such a character. Indeed it is such a rich example that we
shall frequently return to it for inspiration and further examination.
Let R denote the set of all real numbers. In Chapter 1 we deï¬ned
three topologies that can be put on any set: the discrete topology, the
indiscrete topology and the ï¬nite-closed topology. So we know three
topologies that can be put on the set R. Six other topologies on R
were deï¬ned in Exercises 1.1 #5 and #9. In this chapter we describe
a much more important and interesting topology on R which is known
as the Euclidean topology.
An analysis of the Euclidean topology leads us to the notion of
â€œbasis for a topologyâ€. In the study of Linear Algebra we learn that
every vector space has a basis and every vector is a linear combination
of members of the basis. Similarly, in a topological space every open
set can be expressed as a union of members of the basis. Indeed, a set
is open if and only if it is a union of members of the basis.

Topology Without Tears

The Euclidean Topolology on R

We now proceed to describe the open sets and the closed sets in

2.2.2
Deï¬nition.
Let (X,Ï„) be a topological space. A collection
B of open subsets of X is said to be a basis for the topology Ï„ if every
open set is a union of members of B.
If B is a basis for a topology Ï„ on a set X then a subset U of X is
in Ï„ if and only if it is a union of members of B. So B â€œgeneratesâ€ the
topology Ï„ in the following sense: if we are told what sets are members
of B then we can determine the members of Ï„ â€“ they are just all the
sets which are unions of members of B.
2.2.3
Example.

basis for the Euclidean topology on R, by Proposition 2.2.1.

2.2.7

Whenever we use the symbol R2 we mean the plane, and if we

topology is, we mean the plane with the Euclidean topology.
To see that B is indeed a basis for a topology, observe that (i) the
plane is the union of all of the open rectangles, and (ii) the intersection
of any two rectangles is a rectangle. [By â€œrectangleâ€ we mean one with
sides parallel to the axes.] So the conditions of Proposition 2.2.8 are
satisï¬ed and hence B is a basis for a topology.
2.2.10 Remark.
By generalizing Example 2.2.9 we see how to put
a topology on Rn = {âŸ¨x1, x2, . . . , xnâŸ©: xi âˆˆR, i = 1, . . . , n} for each

of Rn with sides parallel to the axes. This collection B is a basis for
the Euclidean topology on Rn.

2.3
Basis for a Given Topology
Proposition 2.2.8 told us under what conditions a collection B of
subsets of a set X is a basis for some topology on X.
However
sometimes we are given a topology Ï„ on X and we want to know
whether B is a basis for this speciï¬c topology Ï„. To verify that B is a
basis for Ï„ we could simply apply Deï¬nition 2.2.2 and show that every
member of Ï„ is a union of members of B. However, Proposition 2.3.2
provides us with an alternative method.
But ï¬rst we present an example which shows that there is a diï¬€er-
ence between saying that a collection B of subsets of X is a basis for

32
Topology Without Tears
2.3.1
Example.
Let B be the collection of all half-open intervals
of the form (a, b], a < b, where (a, b] = {x : x âˆˆR, a < x â‰¤b}. Then
B is a basis for a topology on R, since R is the union of all members
of B and the intersection of any two half-open intervals is a half-open
interval.
However, the topology Ï„1 which has B as its basis, is not the
Euclidean topology on R. We can see this by observing that (a, b] is
an open set in R with topology Ï„1, while (a, b] is not an open set in R
with the Euclidean topology. (See Exercises 2.1 #1.) So B is a basis
for some toplogy but not a basis for the Euclidean topology on R.
2.3.2
Proposition.
Let (X,Ï„) be a topological space. A family
B of open subsets of X is a basis for Ï„ if and only if for any point x
belonging to any open set U, there is a B âˆˆB such that x âˆˆB âŠ†U.
Proof.

x âˆˆB âŠ†U, and
(ii) if for each U âˆˆÏ„ and x âˆˆU there exists a B âˆˆB such that x âˆˆB âŠ†U, then
B is a basis for Ï„.
Assume B is a basis for Ï„ and x âˆˆU âˆˆÏ„. As B is a basis for Ï„,
the open set U is a union of members of B; that is, U =

jâˆˆJ Bj, where
Bj âˆˆB, for each j in some index set J. But x âˆˆU implies x âˆˆBj, for
some j âˆˆJ. Thus x âˆˆBj âŠ†U, as required.
Conversely, assume that for each U âˆˆÏ„ and each x âˆˆU, there
exists a B âˆˆB with x âˆˆB âŠ†U. We have to show that every open set


xâˆˆV Bx.
(Check this!) Thus V is a union of members of B.
2.3.3

Let B be a basis for a topology Ï„ on a set X.
Then a subset U of X is open if and only if for each x âˆˆU there exists

Observe that the basis property described in Proposition 2.3.3 is

Proposition 2.2.8 and Proposition 2.3.2.
Proposition 2.2.8 gives conditions for a family B of subsets of a set
X to be a basis for some topology on X. However, Proposition 2.3.2
gives conditions for a family B of subsets of a topological space (X,Ï„)
to be a basis for the given topology Ï„.
We have seen that a topology can have many diï¬€erent bases. The

2.4
Postscript
In this chapter we have deï¬ned a very important topological space â€“ R,
the set of all real numbers with the Euclidean topology, and spent some
time analyzing it. We observed that, in this topology, open intervals
are indeed open sets (and closed intervals are closed sets). However,
not all open sets are open intervals. Nevertheless, every open set in
R is a union of open intervals. This led us to introduce the notion of
â€œbasis for a topologyâ€ and to establish that the collection of all open
intervals is a basis for the Euclidean topology on R.
In the introduction to Chapter 1 we described a mathematical
proof as a watertight argument and underlined the importance of writ-
ing proofs. In this chapter we introduced proof by contradiction in
Examples 2.1.2 (v) with another example in Example 2.2.7. Proving
â€œnecessary and suï¬ƒcientâ€ conditions, that is, â€œif and only ifâ€ condi-
tions, was explained in Proposition 2.2.1, with further examples in
Propositions 2.2.8, 2.3.2, 2.3.3, and 2.3.4.
Bases for topologies is a signiï¬cant topic in its own right.
We
saw, for example, that the collection of all singletons is a basis for
the discrete topology. Proposition 2.2.8 gives necessary and suï¬ƒcient
conditions for a collection of subsets of a set X to be a basis for some
topology on X. This was contrasted with Proposition 2.3.2 which gives
necessary and suï¬ƒcient conditions for a collection of subsets of X to
be a basis for the given topology on X. It was noted that two diï¬€erent
collections B1 and B2 can be bases for the same topology Necessary

We deï¬ned the Euclidean topology on Rn, for n any positive inte-
ger. We saw that the family of all open discs is a basis for R2, as is
the family of all open squares, or the family of all open rectangles.
The exercises introduced three interesting ideas.
Exercises 2.1
#8 covered the notions of FÏƒ-set and GÎ´-set which are important in
measure theory. Exercises 2.3 #4 introduced the space of continuous
real-valued functions. Such spaces are called function spaces which are
the central objects of study in functional analysis. Functional analysis
is a blend of (classical) analysis and topology, and was for some time
called modern analysis. Finally, Exercises 2.3 #5â€“12 dealt with the
notion of subbasis.

On the real number line we have a notion of â€œclosenessâ€. For example
each point in the sequence .1, .01, .001, .0001, .00001, . . . is closer to 0
than the previous one. Indeed, in some sense, 0 is a limit point of this
sequence. So the interval (0, 1] is not closed, as it does not contain
the limit point 0. In a general topological space we do not have a
â€œdistance functionâ€, so we must proceed diï¬€erently. We shall deï¬ne
the notion of limit point without resorting to distances. Even with our
new deï¬nition of limit point, the point 0 will still be a limit point of
(0, 1] . The introduction of the notion of limit point will lead us to a
much better understanding of the notion of closed set.
Another very important topological concept we shall introduce in
this chapter is that of connectedness. Consider the topological space
R. While the sets [0, 1] âˆª[2, 3] and [4, 6] could both be described as
having length 2, it is clear that they are diï¬€erent types of sets . . . the
ï¬rst consists of two disjoint pieces and the second of just one piece.
The diï¬€erence between the two is â€œtopologicalâ€ and will be exposed
using the notion of connectedness.

3.1
Limit Points and Closure
3.1.1
Deï¬nition.
Let A be a subset of a topological space (X,Ï„).
A point* x âˆˆX is said to be a limit point (or accumulation point or
cluster point) of A if every open set, U, containing x contains a point
of A diï¬€erent from x.
3.1.2
Example.
Consider the topological space (X,Ï„) where the
set X = {a, b, c, d, e}, Ï„ = {X, Ã˜, {a}, {c, d}, {a, c, d}, {b, c, d, e}}, and
A = {a, b, c}. Then b, d, and e are limit points of A but a and c are
not.
Proof.
The point a is a limit point of A if and only if every open set containing a
contains another point of the set A.
So to show that a is not a limit point of A, it suï¬ƒces to ï¬nd even one open
set which contains a but contains no other point of A.
The set {a} is open and contains no other point of A. So a is not
a limit point of A.
The set {c, d} is an open set containing c but no other point of A.
So c is not a limit point of A.
To show that b is a limit point of A, we have to show that every open set
containing b contains a point of A other than b.
We shall show this is the case by writing down all of the open sets containing
b and verifying that each contains a point of A other than b.
The only open sets containing b are X and {b, c, d, e} and both
contain another element of A, namely c. So b is a limit point of A.
The point d is also a limit point of A, even though it is not in
A. This is so since every open set containing d contains a point of A.
Similarly e is a limit point of A even though it is not in A.
* If (X Ï„) is a topological space then it is usual to refer to the elements of the set X as

3.1.3
Example.
Let (X,Ï„) be a discrete space and A a subset of
X. Then A has no limit points, since for each x âˆˆX, {x} is an open
set containing no point of A diï¬€erent from x.
3.1.4
Example.
Consider the subset A = [a, b) of R. Then it is
easily veriï¬ed that every element in [a, b) is a limit point of A. The
point b is also a limit point of A.
3.1.5
Example.
Let (X,Ï„) be an indiscrete space and A a subset
of X with at least two elements. Then it is readily seen that every
point of X is a limit point of A. (Why did we insist that A have at
least two points?)
The next proposition provides a useful way of testing whether a
set is closed or not.
3.1.6
Proposition.
Let A be a subset of a topological space
(X,Ï„). Then A is closed in (X,Ï„) if and only if A contains all of
its limit points.
Proof.
We are required to prove that A is closed in (X,Ï„) if and only if A contains
all of its limit points; that is, we have to show that
(i) if A is a closed set, then it contains all of its limit points, and
(ii) if A contains all of its limit points, then it is a closed set.
Assume that A is closed in (X,Ï„). Suppose that p is a limit point
of A which belongs to X \A. Then X \A is an open set containing the
limit point p of A. Therefore X \ A contains an element of A. This is
clearly false. Therefore every limit point of A must belong to A.
Conversely, assume that A contains all of its limit points.
For
each z âˆˆX \ A, our assumption implies that there exists an open
set Uz âˆ‹z such that Uz âˆ©A = Ã˜; that is, Uz âŠ†X \ A. Therefore

3.1.9

Let A be a subset of a topological space (X,Ï„).

Proof.
To ï¬nd the closure of a particular set, we shall ï¬nd all the closed sets containing
that set and then select the smallest. We therefore begin by writing down all of
the closed sets â€“ these are simply the complements of all the open sets.
The closed sets are Ã˜, X, {b, c, d, e}, {a, b, e}, {b, e} and {a}.
So
the smallest closed set containing {b} is {b, e}; that is, {b} = {b, e}.
Similarly {a, c} = X, and {b, d} = {b, c, d, e}.
3.1.12
Example.
Let Q be the subset of R consisting of all the
rational numbers. Prove that Q = R.
Proof.
Suppose Q Ì¸= R. Then there exists an x âˆˆR \ Q. As R\Q is
open in R, there exist a, b with a < b such that x âˆˆ(a, b) âŠ†R \ Q. But

So q âˆˆR\Q which implies q âˆˆR\Q. This is a contradiction, as q âˆˆQ.
Hence Q = R.

Then every

only dense subset of X is X itself, since each subset of X is its own
closure.

3.2.6
Proposition.
Let A be a subset of a topological space
(X,Ï„). A point x âˆˆX is a limit point of A if and only if every
neighbourhood of x contains a point of A diï¬€erent from x.
As a set is closed if and only if it contains all its limit points we
deduce the following:
3.2.7
Corollary.
Let A be a subset of a topological space (X,Ï„).
Then the set A is closed if and only if for each x âˆˆX \ A there is a
neighbourhood N of x such that N âŠ†X \ A.
3.2.8
Corollary.
Let U be a subset of a topological space (X,Ï„).
Then U âˆˆÏ„ if and only if for each x âˆˆU there exists a neighbourhood
N of x such that N âŠ†U.
The next corollary is readily deduced from Corollary 3.2.8.
3.2.9
Corollary.
Let U be a subset of a topological space (X,Ï„).
Then U âˆˆÏ„ if and only if for each x âˆˆU there exists a V âˆˆÏ„ such
that x âˆˆV âŠ†U.
Corollary 3.2.9 provides a useful test of whether a set is open or
not. It says that a set is open if and only if it contains an open set
about each of its points.

3.3
Connectedness
3.3.1
Remark.
We record here some deï¬nitions and facts you
should already know. Let S be any set of real numbers. If there is an
element b in S such that x â‰¤b, for all x âˆˆS, then b is said to be the
greatest element of S. Similarly if S contains an element a such that
a â‰¤x, for all x âˆˆS, then a is called the least element of S. A set S of
real numbers is said to be bounded above if there exists a real number
c such that x â‰¤c, for all x âˆˆS, and c is called an upper bound for S.
Similarly the terms â€œbounded belowâ€ and â€œlower boundâ€ are deï¬ned. A
set which is bounded above and bounded below is said to be bounded.
Least Upper Bound Axiom:
Let S be a non-empty set of real

Limit Points
49
The least upper bound, also called the supremum of S, may or may
not belong to the set S. Indeed, the supremum of S is an element of
S if and only if S has a greatest element. For example, the supremum
of the open interval S = (1, 2) is 2 but 2 /âˆˆ(1, 2), while the supremum
of [3, 4] is 4 which does lie in [3, 4] and 4 is the greatest element of
[3, 4]. Any set of real numbers which is bounded below has a greatest
lower bound which is also called the inï¬mum.
3.3.2
Lemma.
Let S be a subset of R which is bounded above
and let p be the supremum of S. If S is a closed subset of R, then
p âˆˆS.
Proof.
Suppose p âˆˆR\S. As R\S is open there exist real numbers
a and b with a < b such that p âˆˆ(a, b) âŠ†R \ S. As p is the least
upper bound for S and a < p, it is clear that there exists an x âˆˆS
such that a < x. Also x < p < b, and so x âˆˆ(a, b) âŠ†R \ S. But this is
a contradiction, since x âˆˆS. Hence our supposition is false and p âˆˆS.
3.3.3
Proposition.
Let T be a clopen subset of R. Then either
T = R or T = Ã˜.
Proof.
Suppose T Ì¸= R and T Ì¸= Ã˜. Then there exists an element
x âˆˆT and an element z âˆˆR \ T. Without loss of generality, assume
x < z. Put S = T âˆ©[x, z]. Then S, being the intersection of two closed
sets, is closed. It is also bounded above, since z is obviously an upper
bound. Let p be the supremum of S. By Lemma 3.3.2, p âˆˆS. Since
p âˆˆ[x, z], p â‰¤z. As z âˆˆR \ S, p Ì¸= z and so p < z.
Now T is also an open set and p âˆˆT.
So there exist a and b
in R with a < b such that p âˆˆ(a, b) âŠ†T.
Let t be such that
p < t < min(b, z), where min(b, z) denotes the smaller of b and z.
So t âˆˆT and t âˆˆ[p, z]. Thus t âˆˆT âˆ©[x, z] = S. This is a contradiction
since t > p and p is the supremum of S. Hence our supposition is false
and consequently T = R or T = Ã˜.
3.3.4
Deï¬nition.
Let (X,Ï„) be a topological space. Then it is
said to be connected if the only clopen subsets of X are X and Ã˜.
So restating Proposition 3.3.3 we obtain:

We conclude this section by recording that R2 (and indeed, Rn,
for each n â‰¥1) is a connected space. However the proof is delayed to

Connectedness is a very important property about which we shall

7. Which of the topological spaces of Exercises 1.1 #9 are connected?
3.4
Postscript
In this chapter we have introduced the notion of limit point and shown
that a set is closed if and only if it contains all its limit points.
Proposition 3.1.8 then tells us that any set A has a smallest closed
set A which contains it. The set A is called the closure of A.
A subset A of a topological space (X,Ï„) is said to be dense in
X if A = X.
We saw that Q is dense in R and the set P of all
irrational numbers is also dense in R. We introduced the notion of
neighbourhood of a point and the notion of connected topological
space. We proved an important result, namely that R is connected.
We shall have much more to say about connectedness later.
In the exercises we introduced the notion of interior of a set, this
being complementary to that of closure of a set.

52
Topology Without Tears
BLANK PAGE

In each branch of mathematics it is essential to recognize when two
structures are equivalent. For example two sets are equivalent, as far
as set theory is concerned, if there exists a bijective function which
maps one set onto the other. Two groups are equivalent, known as
isomorphic, if there exists a a homomorphism of one to the other which
is one-to-one and onto. Two topological spaces are equivalent, known
as homeomorphic, if there exists a homeomorphism of one onto the
other.

4.1
Subspaces
4.1.1
Deï¬nition.
Let Y be a non-empty subset of a topological
space (X,Ï„). The collection Ï„Y = {O âˆ©Y : O âˆˆÏ„} of subsets of Y is
a topology on Y called the subspace topology (or the relative topology
or the induced topology or the topology induced on Y by Ï„).
The topological space (Y,Ï„Y ) is said to be a subspace of (X,Ï„).
Of course you should check that TY is indeed a topology on Y .

and Y = {b, c, e}. Then the subspace topology on Y is
Ï„Y = {Y, Ã˜, {c}}.

Let Z be the subset of R consisting of all the

Proof.

So the induced topology is discrete.

4.2
Homeomorphisms
We now turn to the notion of equivalent topological spaces. We begin
by considering an example:
X = {a, b, c, d, e},
Y = {g, h, i, j, k},
Ï„ = {X, Ã˜, {a}, {c, d}, {a, c, d}.{b, c, d, e}}
and
Ï„1 = {Y, Ã˜, {g}, {i, j}, {g, i, j}, {h, i, j, k}}.
It is clear that in an intuitive sense (X,Ï„) is â€œequivalentâ€ to (Y,Ï„1).
The function f: X â†’Y deï¬ned by f(a) = g, f(b) = h, f(c) =
i, f(d) = j, and f(e) = k, provides the equivalence. We now formalize
this.
4.2.1
Deï¬nition.
Let (X,Ï„) and (Y,Ï„1) be topological spaces.
Then they are said to be homeomorphic if there exists a function
f : X â†’Y which has the following properties:
(i) f is one-to-one (that is f(x1) = f(x2) implies x1 = x2),
(ii) f is onto (that is, for any y âˆˆY there exists an x âˆˆX such that
f(x) = y),
(iii) for each U âˆˆÏ„1, f âˆ’1(U) âˆˆÏ„, and
(iv) for each V âˆˆÏ„, f(V ) âˆˆÏ„1.
Further, the map f is said to be a homeomorphism between (X,Ï„) and
(Y,Ï„1). We write (X,Ï„) âˆ¼= (Y,Ï„1).
We shall show that â€œâˆ¼=â€ is an equivalence relation and use this to
show that all open intervals (a, b) are homeomorphic to each other.
Example 4.2.2 is the ï¬rst step, as it shows that â€œâˆ¼=â€ is a transitive
relation.

4.3
Non-Homeomorphic Spaces
To prove two topological spaces are homeomorphic we have to ï¬nd a
homeomorphism between them.
But, to prove that two topological spaces are not homeomorphic is
often much harder as we have to show that no homeomorphism exists.
The following example gives us a clue as to how we might go about
showing this.
4.3.1
Example.
Prove that [0, 2] is not homeomorphic to the
subspace [0, 1] âˆª[2, 3] of R.
Proof.
Let (X,Ï„) = [0, 2] and (Y,Ï„1) = [0, 1] âˆª[2, 3]. Then
[0, 1] = [0, 1] âˆ©Y â‡’[0, 1] is closed in (Y,Ï„1)

64
Topology Without Tears
and
[0, 1] = (âˆ’1, 11/2) âˆ©Y â‡’[0, 1] is open in (Y,Ï„1).
Thus Y is not connected, as it has [0, 1] as a proper non-empty clopen
subset.
Suppose that (X,Ï„) âˆ¼= (Y,Ï„1). Then there exists a homeomor-
phism f: (X,Ï„) â†’(Y,Ï„1).
So f âˆ’1([0, 1]) is a clopen subset of X,
and hence X is not connected.
This is false as [0, 2] = X is con-
nected. (See Exercises 4.1 #15.) So we have a contradiction and thus
(X,Ï„) Ì¸âˆ¼= (Y,Ï„1).
What do we learn from this?
4.3.2
Proposition.
Any topological space homeomorphic to a
connected space is connected.
Proposition 4.3.2 gives us one way to try to show two topological
spaces are not homeomorphic . . . by ï¬nding a property â€œpreserved by
homeomorphismsâ€ which one space has and the other does not.
Amongst the exercises we have met many properties â€œpreserved
by homeomorphismsâ€:
(i) T0-space;
(ii) T1-space;
(iii) T2-space or Hausdorï¬€space;
(iv) regular space;
(v) T3-space;
(vi) satisfying the second axiom of countability;
(vii) separable space. [See Exercises 4.2 #7.]
There are also others:
(viii) discrete space;
(ix) indiscrete space;
(x) ï¬nite-closed topology;
(xi) countable-closed topology.
So together with connectedness we know twelve properties pre-
served by homeomorphisms. Also two spaces (X,Ï„) and (Y,Ï„1) cannot
be homeomorphic if X and Y have diï¬€erent cardinalities or if Ï„ and Ï„1
have diï¬€erent cardinalities, e.g. X is countable and Y is uncountable.

Homeomorphisms
65
Nevertheless when faced with a speciï¬c problem we may not have
the one we need. For example, show that (0, 1) is not homeomorphic
to [0, 1] or show that R is not homeomorphic to R2. We shall see how
to show that these spaces are not homeomorphic shortly.
Before moving on to this let us settle the following question: which
subspaces of R are connected?
4.3.3
Deï¬nition.
A subset S of R is said to be an interval if it
has the following property: if x âˆˆS, z âˆˆS, and y âˆˆR are such that
x < y < z, then y âˆˆS.
4.3.4
Remarks. (i) Note that each singleton set {x} is an interval.
(ii) Every interval has one of the following forms: {a}, [a, b], (a, b),
[a, b), (a, b], (âˆ’âˆ, a), (âˆ’âˆ, a], (a, âˆ), [a, âˆ), (âˆ’âˆ, âˆ).
(iii) It follows from Example 4.2.6, Remark 4.2.7, and Exercises
4.2 #1, that every interval is homeomorphic to (0, 1), [0, 1],
[0, 1), or {0}. In Exercises 4.3 #1 we are able to make an even
stronger statement.
4.3.5
Proposition.
A subspace S of R is connected if and only
if it is an interval.
Proof.
That all intervals are connected can be proved in a similar
fashion to Proposition 3.3.3 by replacing R everywhere in the proof by
the interval we are trying to prove connected.
Conversely, let S be connected. Suppose x âˆˆS, z âˆˆS, x < y < z,
and y /âˆˆS. Then (âˆ’âˆ, y) âˆ©S = (âˆ’âˆ, y] âˆ©S is an open and closed
subset of S. So S has a clopen subset, namely (âˆ’âˆ, y) âˆ©S. To show
that S is not connected we have to verify only that this clopen set is
proper and non-empty. It is non-empty as it contains x. It is proper
as z âˆˆS but z /âˆˆ(âˆ’âˆ, y) âˆ©S.
So S is not connected.
This is a
contradiction. Therefore S is an interval.
We now see a reason for the name â€œconnectedâ€. Subspaces of R
such as [a, b], (a, b), etc. are connected, while subspaces like
X = [0, 1] âˆª[2, 3] âˆª[5, 6]
which is a union of â€œdisconnectedâ€ pieces, are not connected.
Now let us turn to the problem of showing that (0, 1) Ì¸âˆ¼= [0, 1].
Firstly, we present a seemingly trivial observation.

66
Topology Without Tears
4.3.6
Remark.
Let f : (X,Ï„) â†’(Y,Ï„1) be a homeomorphism.
Let a âˆˆX, so that X\{a} is a subspace of X and has induced topology
Ï„2. Also Y \ {f(a)} is a subspace of Y and has induced topology Ï„3.
Then (X \ {a},Ï„2) is homeomorphic to (Y \ {f(a)},Ï„3).
Outline Proof.
Deï¬ne g: X \ {a} â†’Y \ {f(a)} by g(x) = f(x), for
all x âˆˆX \ {a}. Then it is easily veriï¬ed that g is a homeomorphism.
(Write down a proof of this.)
As an immediate consequence of this we have:
4.3.7
Corollary.
If a, b, c, and d are real numbers with a < b and
c < d, then
(i) (a, b) Ì¸âˆ¼= [c, d),
(ii) (a, b) Ì¸âˆ¼= [c, d], and
(iii) [a, b) Ì¸âˆ¼= [c, d].
Proof.
(i)
Let (X,Ï„) = [c, d) and (Y,Ï„1) = (a, b). Suppose that
(X,Ï„) âˆ¼= (Y,Ï„1). Then X \ {c} âˆ¼= Y \ {y}, for some y âˆˆY . But,
X \ {c} = (c, d), an interval, and so is connected, while no matter
which point we remove from (a, b) the resultant space is disconnected.
Hence
X \ {c} Ì¸âˆ¼= Y \ {y}, for each y âˆˆY.
This is a contradiction. So [c, d) Ì¸âˆ¼= (a, b).
(ii)
[c, d] \ {c} is connected, while (a, b) \ {y} is disconnected for
all y âˆˆ(a, b). Thus (a, b) Ì¸âˆ¼= [c, d].
(iii)
Suppose that [a, b) âˆ¼= [c, d]. Then [c, d]\ {c} âˆ¼= [a, b)\ {y} for
some y âˆˆ[a, b). Therefore ([c, d] \ {c}) \ {d} âˆ¼= ([a, b) \ {y}) \ {z}, for
some z âˆˆ[a, b) \ {y}; that is, (c, d) âˆ¼= [a, b) \ {y, z}, for some distinct
y and z in [a, b). But (c, d) is connected, while [a, b) \ {y, z}, for any
two distinct points y and z in [a, b), is disconnected. So we have a
contradiction. Therefore [a, b) Ì¸âˆ¼= [c, d].

4.4
Postscript
There are three important ways of creating new topological spaces
from old ones: forming subspaces, products, and quotient spaces. We
examine all three in due course. Forming subspaces was studied in this
section. This allowed us to introduce the important spaces Q, [a, b],
(a, b), etc.
We deï¬ned the central notion of homeomorphism. We noted that
â€œâˆ¼=â€ is an equivalence relation. A property is said to be topological
if it is preserved by homeomorphisms; that is, if (X, T ) âˆ¼= (Y,Ï„1)
and (X,Ï„) has the property then (Y, T1) must also have the property.
Connectedness was shown to be a topological property. So any space
homeomorphic to a connected space is connected. (A number of other
topological properties were also identiï¬ed.) We formally deï¬ned the
notion of an interval in R, and showed that the intervals are precisely
the connected subspaces of R.

Homeomorphisms
69
Given two topological spaces (X,Ï„) and (Y,Ï„1) it is an interesting
task to show whether they are homeomorphic or not. We proved that
every interval in R is homeomorphic to one and only one of [0, 1],
(0, 1), [0, 1), and {0}.
In the next section we show that R is not
homeomorphic to R2. A tougher problem is to show that R2 is not
homeomorphic to R3. This will be done later via the Jordan curve
theorem. Still the cr`eme de la cr`eme is the fact that Rn âˆ¼= Rm if and
only if n = m. This is best approached via algebraic topology, which
is only touched upon in this book.
Exercises 4.2 #6 introduced the notion of group of homeomor-
phisms, which is an interesting and important topic in its own right.

CHAPTER 5
Continuous Mappings
In most branches of pure mathematics we study what in category the-
ory are called â€œobjectsâ€ and â€œarrowsâ€. In linear algebra the objects
are the vector spaces and the arrows are the linear transformations.
In group theory the objects are groups and the arrows are homomor-
phisms, while in set theory the objects are sets and the arrows are
functions. In topology the objects are the topological spaces. We now
introduce the arrows . . . the continuous mappings.
71

5.1
Continuous Mappings
Of course we are already familiar with the notion of a continuous
function from R into R.*
A function f : R â†’R is said to be continuous if for each a âˆˆR
and each positive real number Îµ, there exists a positive real number Î´
such that | x âˆ’a |< Î´ implies | f(x) âˆ’f(a) |< Îµ.
It is not at all obvious how to generalize this deï¬nition to general
topological spaces where we do not have â€œabsolute valueâ€ or â€œsubtrac-
tionâ€. So we shall seek another (equivalent) deï¬nition of continuity
which lends itself more to generalization.
It is easily seen that f : R â†’R is continuous if and only if for each
a âˆˆR and each interval (f(a) âˆ’Îµ, f(a) + Îµ), for Îµ > 0, there exists a
Î´ > 0 such that f(x) âˆˆ(f(a) âˆ’Îµ , f(a) + Îµ) for all x âˆˆ(a âˆ’Î´ , a + Î´).
This deï¬nition is an improvement since it does not involve the
concept â€œabsolute valueâ€ but it still involves â€œsubtractionâ€. The next
lemma shows how to avoid subtraction.
5.1.1
Lemma.
Let f be a function mapping R into itself. Then
f is continuous if and only if for each a âˆˆR and each open set U
containing f(a), there exists an open set V containing a such that
f(V ) âŠ†U.
Proof.
Assume that f is continuous. Let a âˆˆR and let U be any
open set containing f(a). Then there exist real numbers c and d such
that f(a) âˆˆ(c, d) âŠ†U. Put Îµ equal to the smaller of the two numbers
d âˆ’f(a) and f(a) âˆ’c, so that
(f(a) âˆ’Îµ , f(a) + Îµ) âŠ†U.
As the mapping f is continuous there exists a Î´ > 0 such that f(x) âˆˆ
(f(a) âˆ’Îµ , f(a) + Îµ) for all x âˆˆ(a âˆ’Î´ , a + Î´). Let V be the open set
(a âˆ’Î´ , a + Î´). Then a âˆˆV and f(V ) âŠ†U, as required.
Conversely assume that for each a âˆˆR and each open set U
containing f(a) there exists an open set V containing a such that
f(V ) âŠ†U. We have to show that f is continuous. Let a âˆˆR and

5.2
Intermediate Value Theorem
5.2.1
Proposition.
Let (X,Ï„) and (Y,Ï„1) be topological spaces
and f : (X,Ï„) â†’(Y,Ï„1) surjective and continuous.
If (X,Ï„) is
connected, then (Y,Ï„1) is connected.
Proof.
Suppose (Y,Ï„1) is not connected.
Then it has a clopen
subset U such that U Ì¸= Ã˜ and U Ì¸= Y . Then f âˆ’1(U) is an open set,
since f is continuous, and also a closed set, by Proposition 5.1.9; that
is, f âˆ’1(U) is a clopen subset of X. Now f âˆ’1(U) Ì¸= Ã˜ as f is surjective
and U Ì¸= Ã˜. Also f âˆ’1(U) Ì¸= X, since if it were U would equal Y , by the
surjectivity of f. Thus (X,Ï„) is not connected. This is a contradiction.
Therefore (Y,Ï„1) is connected.
5.2.2
Remarks.
(i)
The above proposition would be false if the
condition â€œsurjectiveâ€ were dropped. (Find an example of this.)
(ii) Simply put, Proposition 5.2.1 says: any continuous image of a
connected set is connected.
(iii) Proposition 5.2.1 tells us that if (X,Ï„) is a connected space
and (Y,Ï„â€²) is not connected (i.e. disconnected) then there exists
no mapping of (X,Ï„) onto (Y,Ï„â€²) which is continuous.
For
example, while there are an inï¬nite number of mappings of
R onto Q (or onto Z), none of them are continuous. Indeed
in Exercise Set 5.2 # 10 we observe that the only continuous
mappings of R into Q (or into Z) are the constant mappings.
The following strengthened version of the notion of connectedness
is often useful.
5.2.3
Deï¬nition.
A topological space (X,Ï„) is said to be path-
connected (or pathwise connected) if for each pair of distinct points a
and b of X there exists a continuous mapping f : [0, 1] â†’(X,Ï„), such
that f(0) = a and f(1) = b. The mapping f is said to be a path joining
a to b.

Continuous Mappings
79
5.2.4
Example.
It is readily seen that every interval is path-
connected.
5.2.5
Example.
For each n â‰¥1, Rn is path-connected.
5.2.6
Proposition.
Every path-connected space is connected.
Proof.
Let (X,Ï„) be a path-connected space and suppose that it
is not connected. Then it has a proper non-empty clopen subset U.
So there exist a and b such that a âˆˆU and b âˆˆX \ U. As (X,Ï„) is
path-connected there exists a continuous function f : [0, 1] â†’(X,Ï„)
such that f(0) = a and f(1) = b. Then f âˆ’1(U) is a clopen subset
of [0, 1].
As a âˆˆU, 0 âˆˆf âˆ’1(U) and so f âˆ’1(U) Ì¸= Ã˜.
As b Ì¸âˆˆU,
1 Ì¸âˆˆf âˆ’1(U) and thus f âˆ’1(U) Ì¸= [0, 1]. Hence f âˆ’1(U) is a proper non-
empty clopen subset of [0, 1], which contradicts the connectedness of
[0, 1]. Consequently (X,Ï„) is connected.
5.2.7
Remark.
The converse of Proposition 5.2.6 is false; that is,
not every connected space is path-connected. An example of such a
space is the following subspace of R2:
X = {âŸ¨x, yâŸ©: y = sin(1/x), 0 < x â‰¤1} âˆª{âŸ¨0, yâŸ©: âˆ’1 â‰¤y â‰¤1}.
[Exercise Set 5.2 #6 shows that X is connected. That X is not path-
connected can be seen by showing that there is no path joining âŸ¨0, 0âŸ©
to, say, the point âŸ¨1/Ï€, 0âŸ©. Draw a picture and try to convince yourself
of this.]
We can now show that R Ì¸âˆ¼= R2.
5.2.8
Example.
Clearly R2\{âŸ¨0, 0âŸ©} is path-connected and hence,
by Proposition 5.2.6, is connected. However R \ {a}, for any a âˆˆR, is
disconnected. Hence R Ì¸âˆ¼= R2.
We now present the Weierstrass Intermediate Value Theorem which
is a beautiful application of topology to the theory of functions of a
real variable. The topological concept crucial to the result is that of
connectedness.

80
Topology Without Tears
5.2.9
Theorem. (Weierstrass Intermediate Value Theorem)
Let f : [a, b] â†’R be continuous and let f(a) Ì¸= f(b). Then for every
number p between f(a) and f(b) there is a point c âˆˆ[a, b] such that
f(c) = p.
Proof.
As [a, b] is connected and f is continuous, Proposition 5.13
says that f([a, b]) is connected. By Proposition 4.3.5 this implies that
f([a, b]) is an interval.
Now f(a) and f(b) are in f([a, b]).
So if p
is between f(a) and f(b), p âˆˆf([a, b]), that is, p = f(c), for some
c âˆˆ[a, b].
5.2.10
Corollary.
If f : [a, b] â†’R is continuous and such that
f(a) > 0 and f(b) < 0, then there exists an x âˆˆ[a, b] such that
f(x) = 0.
5.2.11
Corollary.
(Fixed Point Theorem)
Let f be a con-
tinuous mapping of [0, 1] into [0, 1]. Then there exists a z âˆˆ[0, 1] such
that f(z) = z. (The point z is called a ï¬xed point.)
Proof.
If f(0) = 0 or f(1) = 1, the result is obviously true. Thus
it suï¬ƒces to consider the case when f(0) > 0 and f(1) < 1.
Let
g : [0, 1] â†’R be deï¬ned by g(x) = x âˆ’f(x). Then g is continuous,
g(0) = âˆ’f(0) < 0, and g(1) = 1 âˆ’f(1) > 0.
Consequently, by
Corollary 5.2.10 there exists a z âˆˆ[0, 1] such that g(z) = 0; that
is, z âˆ’f(z) = 0 or f(z) = z.
5.2.12
Remark.
Corollary 5.2.11 is a special case of a very im-
portant theorem called the Brouwer Fixed Point Theorem which says
that if you map an n-dimensional cube continuously into itself then
there is a ï¬xed point. [There are many proofs of this theorem, but
most depend on methods of algebraic topology. An unsophisticated
proof is given on pp. 238â€“239 of the book â€œIntroduction to Set Theory
and Topologyâ€, by K. Kuratowski (Pergamon Press, 1961).]

5.3
Postscript.
In this chapter we said that a mapping** between topological spaces
is called â€œcontinuousâ€ if it has the property that the inverse image of
every open set is an open set. This is an elegant deï¬nition and easy to
understand. It contrasts with the one we meet in real analysis which
was mentioned at the beginning of this section. We have generalized
the real analysis deï¬nition, not for the sake of generalization, but
rather to see what is really going on.
The Weierstrass Intermediate Value Theorem seems intuitively
obvious, but we now see it follows from the fact that R is connected
and that any continuous image of a connected space is connected.
We introduced a stronger property than connected, namely path-
connected.
In many cases it is not suï¬ƒcient to insist that a space
be connected, it must be path-connected.
This property plays an
important role in algebraic topology.
We shall return to the Brouwer Fixed Point Theorem in due course.
It is a powerful theorem. Fixed point theorems play important roles in
various branches of mathematics including topology, functional analy-
sis, and diï¬€erential equations. They are still a topic of research activity
today.
In Exercises 5.2 #9 and #10 we met the notions of â€œcomponentâ€
and â€œtotally disconnectedâ€. Both of these are important for an under-
standing of connectedness.
**
Warning:
Some books use the terms â€œmappingâ€ and â€œmapâ€ to mean continuous
mapping. We do not.

Metric Spaces
The most important class of topological spaces is the class of metric
spaces. Metric spaces provide a rich source of examples in topology.
But more than this, most of the applications of topology to analysis
are via metric spaces.

6.1
Metric Spaces
6.1.1
Deï¬nition.
Let X be a non-empty set and d a real-valued
function deï¬ned on X Ã— X such that for a, b âˆˆX:
(i) d(a, b) â‰¥0 and d(a, b) = 0 if and only if a = b,
(ii) d(a, b) = d(b, a) and
(iii) d(a, c) â‰¤d(a, b) + d(b, c), [the triangle inequality] for all a, b
and c in X.
Then d is said to be a metric on X, (X, d) is called a metric space, and
d(a, b) is referred to as the distance between a and b.

Example.

Deï¬nition.
A space (X,Ï„) is said to be metrizable if there
exists a metric d on the set X with the property that Ï„ is the topology
induced by d.
So, for example, the set Z with the ï¬nite-closed topology is not a
metrizable space.
Warning.
One should not be misled by Proposition 6.1.25 into
thinking that every Hausdorï¬€space is metrizable. Later on we shall
be able to produce (using inï¬nite products) examples of Hausdorï¬€
spaces which are not metrizable. [Metrizability of topological spaces
is quite a technical topic. For necessary and suï¬ƒcient conditions for
metrizability see Theorem 9.1, page 195, of the book â€œTopologyâ€ by
James Dugundji (Allyn and Bacon, 1968).]

6.2
Convergence of Sequences
You are familiar with the notion of a convergent sequence of real
numbers. It is deï¬ned as follows. The sequence x1, x2, . . . , xn, . . . of

100
Topology Without Tears
real numbers is said to converge to the real number x if given any Îµ > 0
there exists an integer n0 such that for all n â‰¥n0, |xn âˆ’x| < Îµ.
It is obvious how this deï¬nition can be extended from R with the
Euclidean metric to any metric space.
6.2.1
Deï¬nitions.
Let (X, d) be a metric space and x1, . . . , xn, . . .
a sequence of points in X. Then the sequence is said to converge to
x âˆˆX if given any Îµ > 0 there exists an integer n0 such that for all
n â‰¥n0, d(x, xn) < Îµ. We denote this by xn â†’x.
The sequence y1, y2, . . . , yn, . . . of points in (X, d) is said to be
convergent if there exist a point y âˆˆX such that yn â†’y.
The next proposition is easily proved, so its proof is left as an
exercise.
6.2.2
Proposition.
Let x1, x2, . . . , xn, . . . be a sequence of points
in a metric space (X, d). Further, let x and y be points in (X, d) such
that xn â†’x and xn â†’y. Then x = y.

Metric Spaces
101
The following proposition tells us the surprising fact that the
topology of a metric space can be described entirely in terms of its
convergent sequences.
6.2.3
Proposition.
Let (X, d) be a metric space. A subset A of
X is closed in (X, d) if and only if every convergent sequence of points
in A converges to a point in A. (In other words, A is closed in (X, d) if
and only if an â†’x, where x âˆˆX and an âˆˆA for all n, implies x âˆˆA.)
Proof.
Assume that A is closed in (X, d) and let an â†’x, where
an âˆˆA for all positive integers n. Suppose that x âˆˆX \ A. Then, as
X \A is an open set containing x, there exists an open ball BÎµ(x) such
that x âˆˆBÎµ(x) âŠ†X \ A. Noting that each an âˆˆA, this implies that
d(x, an) > Îµ for each n. Hence the sequence a1, a2, . . . , an, . . . does not
converge to x. This is a contradiction. So x âˆˆA, as required.
Conversely, assume that every convergent sequence of points in A
converges to a point of A. Suppose that X \A is not open. Then there
exists a point y âˆˆX \ A such that for each Îµ > 0, BÎµ(y) âˆ©A Ì¸= Ã˜. For
each positive integer n, let xn be any point in B1/n(y) âˆ©A. Then we
claim that xn â†’y. To see this let Îµ be any positive real number, and
n0 any integer greater than 1/Îµ. Then for each n â‰¥n0,
xn âˆˆB1/n(y) âŠ†B1/n0(y) âŠ†BÎµ(y).
So xn â†’y and, by our assumption, y âˆˆX \ A. This is a contradiction
and so X \ A is open and thus A is closed in (X, d).

Topology Without Tears
Having seen that the topology of a metric space can be described
in terms of convergent sequences, we should not be surprised that
continuous functions can also be so described.
6.2.4
Proposition.
Let (X, d) and (Y, d1) be metric spaces and
f a mapping of X into Y . Let Ï„ and Ï„1 be the topologies determined
by d and d1, respectively. Then f : (X,Ï„) â†’(Y,Ï„1) is continuous if
and only if xn â†’x â‡’f(xn) â†’f(x); that is, if x1, x2, . . . , xn, . . . is
a sequence of points in (X, d) converging to x, then the sequence of
points f(x1), f(x2), . . . , f(xn), . . . in (Y, d1) converges to f(x).
Proof.
Assume that xn â†’x â‡’f(xn) â†’f(x). To verify that f is
continuous it suï¬ƒces to show that the inverse image of every closed
set in (Y,Ï„1) is closed in (X,Ï„). So let A be closed in (Y,Ï„1). Let
x1, x2, . . . , xn, . . . be a sequence of points in f âˆ’1(A) convergent to a
point x âˆˆX. As xn â†’x, f(xn) â†’f(x). But since each f(xn) âˆˆA
and A is closed, Proposition 6.2.3 then implies that f(x) âˆˆA. Thus
x âˆˆf âˆ’1(A).
So we have shown that every convergent sequence of
points from f âˆ’1(A) converges to a point of f âˆ’1(A). Thus f âˆ’1(A) is
closed, and hence f is continuous.
Conversely, let f be continuous and xn â†’x. Let Îµ be any positive
real number. Then the open ball BÎµ(f(x)) is an open set in (Y,Ï„1).
Therefore f âˆ’1(BÎµ(f(x)) is an open set in (X,Ï„) and it contains x.
Therefore there exists a Î´ > 0 such that
x âˆˆBÎ´(x) âŠ†f âˆ’1(BÎµ(f(x))).
As xn â†’x, there exists a positive integer n0 such that for all n â‰¥n0,
xn âˆˆBÎ´(x). Therefore
f(xn) âˆˆf(BÎ´(x)) âŠ†BÎµ(f(x)), for all n â‰¥n0.
Thus f(xn) â†’f(x).
The corollary below is easily deduced from Proposition 6.2.3
6.2.5
Corollary.
Let (X, d) and (Y, d1) be metric spaces, f a
mapping of X into Y , and Ï„ and Ï„1 the topologies determined by
d and d1, respectively. Then f : (X,Ï„) â†’(Y,Ï„1) is continuous if and
only if for each x0 âˆˆX and Îµ > 0, there exists a Î´ > 0 such that x âˆˆX
and d(x, x0) < Î´ â‡’d1(f(x), f(x0)) < Îµ.

6.3
Postscript
Metric space theory is an important topic in its own right. As well
metric spaces hold an important position in the study of topology. In-
deed many books on topology begin with metric spaces, and motivate
the study of topology via them.
We saw that diï¬€erent metrics on the same set can give rise to the
same topology. Such metrics are called equivalent metrics. We were
introduced to the study of function spaces, and in particular, C[0, 1].
En route we met normed vector spaces, a central topic in functional
analysis.
Not all topological spaces arise from metric spaces. We saw this
by observing that topologies induced by metrics are Hausdorï¬€.
We saw that the topology of a metric space can be described en-
tirely in terms of its convergent sequences and that continuous func-
tions between metric spaces can also be so described.
Exercises 6.2 #9 introduced the interesting concept of distance
between sets in a metric space.

CHAPTER 7
Compactness
The most important topological property is compactness. It plays a
key role in many branches of mathematics. It would be fair to say that
until you understand compactness you do not understand topology!
So what is compactness? It could be described as the topologists
generalization of ï¬niteness. The formal deï¬nition says that a topolog-
ical space is compact if whenever it is a subset of a union of an inï¬nite
number of open sets then it is also a subset of a union of a ï¬nite num-
ber of these open sets. Obviously every ï¬nite subset of a topological
space is compact. And we quickly see that in a discrete space a set is
compact if and only if it is ï¬nite. When we move to topological spaces
with richer topological structures, such as R, we discover that inï¬nite
sets can be compact. Indeed all closed intervals [a, b] in R are compact.
But intervals of this type are the only ones which are compact.
So we are led to ask: precisely which subsets of R are compact?
The Heine-Borel Theorem will tell us that the compact subsets of R
are precisely the sets which are both closed and bounded.
As we go farther into our study of topology, we shall see that
compactness plays a crucial role. This is especially so of applications
of topology to analysis.

Topology Without Tears

7.2.2
Corollary.
Let (X,Ï„) and (Y,Ï„1) be homeomorphic topo-

7.2.3
Corollary.
For a and b in R with a < b, [a, b] is compact
while (a, b) is not compact.
Proof.
The space [a, b] is homeomorphic to the compact space [0, 1]
and so, by Proposition 7.2.1, is compact.
The space (a, b) is homeomorphic to (0, âˆ). If (a, b) were compact,
then (0, âˆ) would be compact, but we saw in Example 7.1.2 that (0, âˆ)
is not compact. Hence (a, b) is not compact.

7.2.5
Proposition.
A compact subset of a Hausdorï¬€topological

Proof.

7.2.6
Corollary.
A compact subset of a metrizable space is closed.
7.2.7
Example.
For a and b in R with a < b, the intervals
[a, b) and (a, b] are not compact as they are not closed subsets of the
metrizable space R.

7.2.8
Proposition.
A compact subset of R is bounded.
Proof.
Let A âŠ†R be unbounded.
Then A âŠ†

7.2.9
Theorem. (Heine-Borel Theorem) Every closed bounded
subset of R is compact.
Proof.
If A is a closed bounded subset of R, then A âŠ†[a, b], for
some a and b in R. As [a, b] is compact and A is a closed subset, A is
compact.
The Heine-Borel Theorem is an important result. The proof above
is short only because we extracted and proved Proposition 7.1.9 ï¬rst.
7.2.10
Proposition.
(Converse of Heine-Borel Theorem)
Every compact subset of R is closed and bounded.
Proof.
This follows immediately from Proposition 7.2.8 and 7.2.5.
7.2.11
Deï¬nition.
A subset A of a metric space (X, d) is said to
be bounded if there exists a real number r such that d(a1, a2) â‰¤r, for
all a1 and a2 in A.
