7.2.12
Proposition.
Let A be a compact subset of a metric space
(X, d). Then A is closed and bounded.
Proof.
By Corollary 7.2.6, A is a closed set. Now ï¬x x0 âˆˆX and
deï¬ne the mapping f : (A,Ï„) â†’R by
f(a) = d(a, x0), for every a âˆˆA,
where Ï„ is the induced topology on A. Then f is continuous and so,
by Proposition 7.2.1, f(A) is compact. Thus, by Proposition 7.2.10,
f(A) is bounded; that is, there exists a real number M such that
f(a) â‰¤M, for all a âˆˆA.
Thus d(a, x0) â‰¤M, for all a âˆˆA. Putting r = 2M, we see by the
triangle inequality that d(a1, a2) â‰¤r, for all a1 and a2 in A.
Recalling that Rn denotes the n-dimensional Euclidean space with
the topology induced by the Euclidean metric, it is possible to gener-
alize the Heine-Borel Theorem and its converse from R to Rn, n > 1.
We state the result here but delay its proof until the next chapter.
7.2.13
Theorem.
(Generalized Heine-Borel Theorem)
A
subset of Rn, n â‰¥1, is compact if and only if it is closed and bounded.
Warning.
Although Theorem 7.2.13 says that every closed bounded
subset of Rn is compact, closed bounded subsets of other metric spaces
need not be compact. (See Exercises 7.2 #9.)

7.2.14
Proposition.
Let (X,Ï„) be a compact space and f a
continuous mapping from (X,Ï„) into R.
Then the set f(X) has a
greatest element and a least element.
Proof.
As f is continuous, f(X) is compact. Therefore f(X) is a
closed bounded subset of R. As f(X) is bounded, it has a supremum.
Since f(X) is closed, Lemma 3.3.2 implies that the supremum is in
f(X).
Thus f(X) has a greatest element â€“ namely its supremum.
Similarly it can be shown that f(X) has a least element.
7.2.15
Proposition.
Let a and b be in R and f a continuous
function from [a, b] into R. Then f([a, b]) = [c, d], for some c and d in
R.
Proof.
As [a, b] is connected, f([a, b]) is a connected subset of R
and hence is an interval. As [a, b] is compact, f([a, b]) is compact. So
f([a, b]) is a closed bounded interval. Hence
f([a, b]) = [c, d]
for some c and d in R.

7.3
Postscript
Compactness plays a key role in applications of topology to all branches
of analysis. As noted in Remark 7.1.4 it can be thought as a topological
generalization of ï¬niteness.
The Generalized Heine-Borel Theorem characterizes the compact
subsets of Rn as those which are closed and bounded.
Compactness is a topological property.
Indeed any continuous
image of a compact space is compact.
Closed subsets of compact spaces are compact and compact sub-
spaces of Hausdorï¬€spaces are closed.
Exercises 7.2 # 5 introduces the notions of open mappings and
closed mappings. Exercises 7.2 #10 notes that a compact Hausdorï¬€
space is a normal space (indeed a T4-space). That the closed unit ball
in each Rn is compact contrasts with Exercises 7.2 #9. This exercise
points out that the closed unit ball in the metric space (C[0, 1], dâˆ—) is
not compact. Though we shall not prove it here, it can be shown that
a normed vector space is ï¬nite-dimensional if and only if its closed unit
ball is compact.
Warning.
It is unfortunate that â€œcompactâ€ is deï¬ned in diï¬€erent
ways in diï¬€erent books and some of these are not equivalent to the
deï¬nition presented here.
Firstly some books include Hausdorï¬€in
the deï¬nition of compact. Some books, particularly older ones, use
â€œcompactâ€ to mean a weaker property than oursâ€”what is often called
sequentially compact. Finally the term â€œbikompaktâ€ is often used to
mean compact or compact Hausdorï¬€in our sense.

CHAPTER 8
Finite Products
There are three important ways of creating new topological spaces
from old ones. They are by forming â€œsubspacesâ€, â€œquotient spacesâ€,
and â€œproduct spacesâ€.
The next three chapters are devoted to the
study of product spaces. In this chapter we investigate ï¬nite products
and prove Tychonoï¬€â€™s Theorem. This seemingly innocuous theorem
says that any product of compact spaces is compact.

8.1
The Product Topology
If X1, X2, . . . , Xn are sets then the product
X1 Ã— X2 Ã— . . . Ã— Xn is
the set consisting of all the ordered n-tuples âŸ¨x1, x2 . . . , xnâŸ©, where
xi âˆˆXi, i = 1, . . . , n. The problem we now discuss is: Given topological
spaces (X1,Ï„1), (X2,Ï„2), . . . , (Xn,Ï„n) how do we deï¬ne a reasonable
topology Ï„ on the product set X1 Ã— X2 Ã— . . . Ã— Xn? An obvious (but
incorrect!) candidate for Ï„ is the set of all sets O1 Ã— O2 Ã— . . . Ã— On,
where Oi âˆˆÏ„i, i = 1, . . . , n. Unfortunately this is not a topology.
For example, if n = 2 and (X, T1) = (X,Ï„2) = R then Ï„ would
contain the rectangles (0, 1) Ã— (0, 1) and (2, 3) Ã— (2, 3) but not the set
[(0, 1)Ã—(0, 1)]âˆª[(2, 3)Ã—(2, 3)], since this is not O1Ã—O2 for any choice of
O1 and O2. [If it were O1Ã—O2 for some O1 and O2, then 1
2 âˆˆ(0, 1) âŠ†O1
and 21

2, 21

âŸ¨1

2âŸ©/âˆˆ[(0, 1) Ã— (0, 1)] âˆª[(2, 3) Ã— (2, 3)].] Thus Ï„ is not closed under
unions and so is not a topology.
However we have already seen how to put a topology (the usual
topology) on R2 = R Ã— R. This was done in Example 2.2.9. Indeed

8.1.1
Deï¬nitions.
Let (X1,Ï„1), (X2,Ï„2), . . . , (Xn,Ï„n) be topo-
logical spaces. Then the product topology Ï„ on the set X1Ã—X2Ã—. . .Ã—Xn
is the topology having the family {O1 Ã— O2 Ã— . . . On, Oi âˆˆÏ„i, i =
1, . . . , n} as a basis. The set X1 Ã—X2 Ã—. . .Ã—Xn with the topology Ï„ is
said to be the product of the spaces (X1,Ï„1), (X2,Ï„2), . . . , (Xn,Ï„n) and
is denoted by (X1Ã—X2Ã—. . . Xn,Ï„) or (X1,Ï„1)Ã—(X2,Ï„2)Ã—. . .Ã—(Xn,Ï„n).
Of course it must be veriï¬ed that the family {O1 Ã— O2 Ã— . . . Ã— On :
Oi âˆˆÏ„i, i = 1, . . . , n} is a basis for a topology; that is, it satisï¬es the
conditions of Proposition 2.2.8. (This is left as an exercise for you.)
8.1.2
Proposition.
Let B1, B2, . . . , Bn be bases for topological
spaces (X1,Ï„1), (X2,Ï„2), . . . , (Xn,Ï„n), respectively. Then the family
{O1 Ã— O2 Ã— . . . Ã— On : Oi âˆˆBi, i = 1, . . . , n} is a basis for the product
topology on X1 Ã— X2 Ã— . . . Ã— Xn.
The proof of Proposition 8.1.2 is straightforward and is also left
as an exercise for you.

8.2
Projections onto Factors of a Product
Before proceeding to our next result we need a couple of deï¬nitions.
8.2.1
Deï¬nitions.
Let Ï„1 and Ï„2 be topologies on a set X. Then
Ï„1 is said to be a ï¬ner topology than Ï„2 (and Ï„2 is said to be a coarser
topology than Ï„1) if Ï„1 âŠ‡Ï„2.
8.2.2
Example.
The discrete topology on a set X is ï¬ner than
any other topology on X. The indiscrete topology on X is coarser
than any other topology on X. [See also Exercises 5.1 #10.]
8.2.3
Deï¬nitions.
Let (X,Ï„) and (Y,Ï„1) be topological spaces
and f a mapping from X into Y . Then f is said to be an open mapping
if for every A âˆˆT , f(A) âˆˆÏ„1. The mapping f is said to be a closed
mapping if for every closed set B in (X,Ï„), f(B) is closed in (Y,Ï„1).
8.2.4
Remark.
In Exercises 7.2 #5, you were asked to show
that none of the conditions â€œcontinuous mappingâ€, â€œopen mappingâ€,
â€œclosed mappingâ€, implies either of the other two conditions. Indeed
no two of these conditions taken together implies the third.
(Find
examples to verify this.)

8.3
Tychonoï¬€â€™s Theorem for Finite Products
We now proceed to state and prove the very important Tychonoï¬€
Theorem. (This is actually Tychonoï¬€â€™s Theorem for ï¬nite products.
The generalization to inï¬nite products is proved in Chapter 10.)
8.3.1
Theorem.
(Tychonoï¬€â€™s Theorem)
If (X1, T1), (X2, T2),
. . . , (Xn,Ï„n) are compact spaces then
n
i=1(Xi,Ï„i) is a compact space.
Proof.
Consider ï¬rst the product of two compact spaces (X,Ï„1)
and (Y,Ï„2). Let Ui, i âˆˆI be any opening covering of X Ã— Y . Then
for each x âˆˆX and y âˆˆY , there exists an i âˆˆI such that âŸ¨x, yâŸ©âˆˆUi.
So there is a basic open set V (x, y) Ã— W(x, y), such that V âŸ¨x, yâŸ©âˆˆÏ„1,
W(x, y) âˆˆÏ„2 and
âŸ¨x, yâŸ©âˆˆV (x, y) Ã— W(x, y) âŠ†Ui.
As âŸ¨x, yâŸ©ranges over all points of X Ã— Y we obtain an open covering

W(x, y) is a subset of some Ui, i âˆˆI. Thus to prove (X,Ï„1) Ã— (Y,Ï„2)

Now ï¬x x0 âˆˆX and consider the subspace {x0} Ã— Y of X Ã— Y .

and so is compact. As V (x0, y) Ã— W(x0, y), y âˆˆY , is an open covering
of {x0} Ã— Y it has a ï¬nite subcovering.
V (x0, y1)Ã—W(x0, y1), V (x0, y2)Ã—W(x0, y2), . . . , V (x0, ym)Ã—W(x0, ym).
Put V (x0) = V (x0, y1) âˆ©V (x0, y2) âˆ©. . . âˆ©V (x0, ym). Then we see that

the form V (x)Ã—Y . As each V (x) is an open set containing x âˆˆX, the

8.3.3
Theorem.
(Generalized Heine-Borel Theorem)
A sub-

Proof.

any compact subset of Rn is closed and bounded.

n terms

8.4
Products and Connectedness
8.4.1
Deï¬nition.
Let (X,Ï„) be a topological space x any point
in X. The component in X of x, CX(x), is deï¬ned to be the union of
all connected subsets of X which contain x.
8.4.2
Proposition.
Let x be any point in a topological space
(X,Ï„). Then CX(x) is connected.
Proof.
Let {Ci : i âˆˆI} be the family of all connected subsets of
(X,Ï„) which contain x. (Observe that {x} âˆˆ{Ci : i âˆˆI}.) Then
CX(x) =

iâˆˆI Ci. Let O be a subset of CX(x) which is clopen in the
topology induced on CX(x) by Ï„. Then Oâˆ©Ci is clopen in the induced
topology on Ci, for each i. But as each Ci is connected, O âˆ©Ci = Ci
or Ã˜, for each i. If O âˆ©Cj = Cj for some j âˆˆI, then x âˆˆO. So, in
this case, O âˆ©Ci Ì¸= Ã˜, for all i âˆˆI as each Ci contains x. Therefore
Oâˆ©Ci = Ci, for all i âˆˆI or Oâˆ©Ci = Ã˜, for all i âˆˆI; that is, O = CX(x)
or O = Ã˜. So CX(x) has no proper non-empty clopen subset and hence
is connected.
8.4.3
Remark.
We see from Deï¬nition 8.4.1 and Proposition 8.4.2
that CX(x) is the largest connected subset of X which contains x.
8.4.4
Lemma.
Let a and b be points in a topological space (X,Ï„).
If there exists a connected set C containing both a and b then CX(a) =
CX(b).
Proof.
By Deï¬nition 8.4.1, CX(a) âŠ‡C and CX(b) âŠ‡C. Therefore
a âˆˆCX(b).
By Proposition 8.4.2, CX(b) and so is a connected set
containing a. Thus, by Deï¬nition 8.4.1, CX(a) âŠ‡CX(b). Similarly
CX(b) âŠ‡CX(a), and we have shown that CX(a) = CX(b).

8.5
Fundamental Theorem of Algebra
In this section we give an application of topology to another branch of
mathematics. We show how to use compactness and the Generalized
Heine-Borel Theorem to prove the Fundamental Theorem of Algebra.

8.6
Postscript
As mentioned in the introduction, this is one of three sections devoted
to the study of product spaces. The easiest case is the one we have just
completed â€“ ï¬nite products. In the next section we study countably
inï¬nite products and in Chapter 10, the general case.
The most
important result proved in this section is Tychonoï¬€â€™s Theorem.* In
Chapter 10 this is generalized to arbitrary sized products.
The second result we called a theorem here is the Generalized
Heine-Borel Theorem which characterizes the compact subsets of Rn
as those which are closed and bounded.
Exercises 8.4 #5 introduced the notion of topological group, that
is a set with the structure of both a topological space and a group, and
with the two structures related in an appropriate manner. Topological
group theory is a rich and interesting branch of mathematics. Exercises
8.3 #1 introduced the notion of locally compact topological space.
Such spaces play a central role in topological group theory.
Our study of connectedness has been furthered in this section by
deï¬ning the component of a point. This allows us to partition any
topological space into connected sets. In a connected space like Rn the
component of any point is the whole space. At the other end of the
scale, the components in any totally disconnected space, for example,
Q, are all singleton sets.
As mentioned above, compactness has a local version.
So too
does connectedness. Exercises 8.4 #6 deï¬ned locally connected. Note,
however, that while every compact space is locally compact, not every
connected space is locally connected. Indeed many properties P have
local versions called locally P, and P usually does not imply locally
P and locally P usually does not imply P.
At the end of the chapter we gave a topological proof of the
Fundamental Theorem of Algebra. Hopefully the fact that a theorem
in one branch of mathematics can be proved using methods from
another branch will suggest to you that mathematics should not be
compartmentalized. While you may have separate courses on algebra,
topology, complex analysis, and number theory these topics are, in
fact, interrelated.
* You should have noticed how sparingly we use the word â€œtheoremâ€, so when we do use that
term it is because the result is important.

147
In the next section we are faced with a problem â€“ how do extend
our deï¬nition of product space to countable products? As pointed out
in Remark 8.2.6, Proposition 8.2.5 provides the bridge to the case of
inï¬nite products.
For those who know some category theory, we observe that the cat-
egory of topological spaces and continuous mappings has both products
and coproducts. The products in the category are indeed the products
of the topological spaces. You may care to identify the coproducts.

Intuition tells us that a curve has zero area.
Thus you should be
astonished to learn of the existence of space-ï¬lling curves. We attack
this topic using the curious space known as the Cantor Space.
It
is surprising that an examination of this space leads us to a better
understanding of the properties of the unit interval [0, 1].

9.2.2
Remark.
Why do we choose to deï¬ne the product topology

we obtain Tychonoï¬€â€™s Theorem (for inï¬nite products), which says that
any product of compact spaces is compact. And this result is extremely
important for applications.

As indicated earlier, we shall in due course prove that any product

9.4
Urysohnâ€™s Theorem
9.4.1
Deï¬nition.
A topological space (X,Ï„) is said to be sepa-
rable if it has a countable dense subset. (See Exercises 3.2 #4 and
Exercises 8.1 #9.)
9.4.2
Example.
Q is dense in R, and so R is separable.
9.4.3
Example.
Every countable topological space is separable.
9.4.4
Proposition.
Let (X,Ï„) be a compact metrizable space.
Then (X,Ï„) is separable.
Proof.
Let d be a metric on X which induces the topology T . For
each positive integer n, let Sn be the family of all open balls having
centres in X and radius 1
n. Then Sn is an open covering of X and so
there is a ï¬nite subcovering Un = {Un1, Un2, . . . , Unk}, for some nk. Let
ynj be the centre of Unj, j = 1, . . . , k, and Yn = {yn1, yn2, . . . , ynk}. Put
Y =
âˆ
n=1 Yn. Then Y is a countable subset of X. We now show that
Y is dense in (X,Ï„).
If V is any non-empty open set in (X,Ï„), then for any v âˆˆV , V
contains an open ball, B, of radius 1
n, about v, for some n. As Un is
an open cover of X, v âˆˆUnj, for some j. Thus d(v, ynj) < 1
n and so
ynj âˆˆB âŠ†V . Hence V âˆ©Y Ì¸= Ã˜, and so Y is dense in X.
9.4.5
Corollary.
The Hilbert cube is a separable space.
We shall prove shortly the very striking Urysohn Theorem which
shows that every compact metrizable space is homeomorphic to a
subspace of the Hilbert cube. En route we prove the (countable version
of the) Embedding Lemma.
First we record the following proposition, which is Exercises 9.3
#3 and so its proof is not included here.

9.4.7

Let (Yi,Ï„i), i =

9.4.8
Deï¬nition.
A topological space (X,Ï„) is said to be a T1-space
if every singleton set {x}, x âˆˆX, is a closed set.
9.4.9
Remark.
It is easily veriï¬ed that every Hausdorï¬€space (i.e.
T2-space) is a T1-space. The converse, however, is false. (See Exercises
4.1 #13 and Exercises 1.3 #3.) In particular, every metrizable space
is a T1-space.
9.4.10
Corollary.
If (X,Ï„) in Lemma 9.4.9 is a T1-space, then
condition (ii) is implied by condition (iii) (and so is superï¬‚uous).
Proof.
Let x1 and x2 be any distinct points in X.
Putting A

fi(x1) /âˆˆ{fi(x2)}. Hence fi(xi) Ì¸= fi(x2), and condition (ii) is satisï¬ed.

9.4.11
Theorem.
(Urysohnâ€™s Theorem)
Every separable metric
space (X, d) is homeomorphic to a subspace of the Hilbert cube.
Proof.
By Corollary 9.4.10 this result will follow if we can ï¬nd a
countably inï¬nite family of mappings fi: (X, d) âˆ’â†’[0, 1], which are (i)
continuous, and (ii) separate points and closed sets.
Without loss of generality we can assume that d(a, b) â‰¤1, for all
a and b in X, since every metric is equivalent to such a metric.
As (X, d) is separable, there exists a countable dense subset Y =
{y1, y2, . . .}. For each i âˆˆ{1, 2, . . . , } deï¬ne fi: X âˆ’â†’[0, 1] by fi(x) =
d(x, yi). It is clear that each mapping fi is continuous.
To see that the mappings {fi} separate points and closed sets, let
x âˆˆX and A be any closed set not containing x. Now X \A is an open
set about x and so contains an open ball B of radius Îµ and centre x,
for some Îµ > 0. Further, as Y is dense in X, there exists a yn such that
d(yn, x) < Îµ
2. Thus d(yn, a) â‰¥Îµ
2, for all a âˆˆA. So [0, Îµ/2] is an open
set in [0, 1] which contains fn(x) but contains no point of A. Hence
fn(x) /âˆˆfn(A) and thus the family {fi} separates points and closed
sets.
9.4.12
Corollary.
Every compact metrizable space is homeo-
morphic to a closed subspace of the Hilbert cube.
9.4.13
Corollary.

Our next task is to verify the converse of Urysohnâ€™s Theorem. To
do this we introduce a new concept. (See Exercises 2.2 #4.)
9.4.14
Deï¬nition.
A topological space (X,Ï„) is said to satisfy
the second axiom of countability (or to be second countable) if there
exists a basis B for Ï„ such that B consists of only a countable number
of sets.
9.4.15
Example.
Let B = {(qâˆ’1
n, q+ 1
n) : q rational, n = 1, 2, . . .}.
Then B is a basis for the usual topology on R. (Verify this). Therefore
R is second countable.
9.4.16
Example.
Let (X,Ï„) be an uncountable set with the dis-
crete topology. Then as every singleton set must be in any basis for
Ï„, (X,Ï„) does not have any countable basis. So (X,Ï„) is not second
countable.
9.4.17
Proposition.
Let (X, d) be a metric space and Ï„ the
corresponding topology. Then (X,Ï„) is a separable space if and only
if it satisï¬es the second axiom of countability.
Proof.
Let (X,Ï„) be separable.
Then it has a countable dense
subset Y = {y1, y2, . . .}. Let B consist of all the open balls (in the
metric d) with centre yi, for some i, and radius 1
n, for some positive
integer n. Clearly B is countable and we shall show that it is a basis
for Ï„.
Let V âˆˆÏ„. Then for any v âˆˆV , V contains an open ball, B,
of radius 1
n about v, for some n. As Y is dense in X, there exists a
ym âˆˆY , such that d(ym, v) <
1
2n. Let Bâ€² be the open ball with centre

1

Also Bâ€² âˆˆB. Hence B is a basis for Ï„. So (X,Ï„) is second countable.
Conversely let (X,Ï„) be second countable, having a countable basis
B1 = {B1, B2, . . .}. For each Bi Ì¸= Ã˜, let bi be any element of Bi, and
put Z equal to the set of all such bi. Then Z is a countable set. Further,
if V âˆˆÏ„, then V âŠ‡Bi, for some i, and so bi âˆˆV. Thus V âˆ©Z Ì¸= Ã˜.
Hence Z is dense in X. Consequently (X, T ) is separable.
9.4.18
Remark.
The above proof shows that any second count-
able space is separable, even without the assumption of metrizability.
However, it is not true, in general, that a separable space is second
countable. (See Exercises 9.4 #11.)

Topology Without Tears
9.4.19
Theorem.
(Urysohnâ€™s Theorem and its converse)
Let
(X, T ) be a topological space. Then (X,Ï„) is separable and metrizable
if and only if it is homeomorphic to a subspace of the Hilbert cube.
Proof.
If (X,Ï„) is separable and metrizable, then Urysohnâ€™s The-
orem 9.4.11 says that it is homeomorphic to a subspace of the Hilbert
cube.
Conversely, let (X,Ï„) be homeomorphic to the subspace (Y,Ï„1)
of the Hilbert cube Iâˆ. By Proposition 9.4.4, Iâˆis separable. So,
by Proposition 9.4.17, it is second countable.
It is readily veriï¬ed
(Exercises 4.1 #14) that any subspace of a second countable space
is second countable, and hence (Y,Ï„1) is second countable. It is also
easily veriï¬ed (Exercises 6.1 #6) that any subspace of a metrizable
space is metrizable. As the Hilbert cube is metrizable, by Corollary
9.3.10, its subspace (Y,Ï„1) is metrizable. So (Y,Ï„1) is metrizable and
satisï¬es the second axiom of countability. Therefore it is separable.
Hence (X,Ï„) is also separable and metrizable.

Corollary.
Let (X,Ï„) be a Hausdorï¬€space. Then it is a

Finally in this chapter we turn to space-ï¬lling curves.
9.5.8
Remark.
Everyone thinks he (or she) knows what a â€œcurveâ€

next theorem shows that there exists a continuous mapping of [0, 1]
onto the product space [0, 1] Ã— [0, 1].
9.5.9
Theorem.
(Peano)
For each positive integer n, there
exists a continuous mapping Ïˆn of [0, 1] onto the n-cube In.
Proof.

We conclude this chapter by stating (but not proving) the
Hahn-Mazurkiewicz Theorem which characterizes those Hausdorï¬€spaces
which are continuous images of [0,1]. [For a proof of the theorem see

9.5.10
Deï¬nition.
A topological space (X,Ï„) is said to be locally
connected if it has a basis of connected (open) sets.
9.5.11
Remark.
Every discrete space is locally connected as are
Rn and Sn, for all n â‰¥1. However, not every connected space is locally
connected. (See Exercises 8.4 #6.)
9.5.12
Theorem.
(Hahn-Mazurkiewicz Theorem)
Let (X,Ï„)
be a Hausdorï¬€space. Then (X,Ï„) is a continuous image of [0, 1] if and
only if it is compact, connected, locally connected, and metrizable.

9.6
Postscript
In this section we have extended the notion of a product of a ï¬nite
number of topological spaces to that of the product of a countable
number of topological spaces. While this step is a natural one, it has
led us to a rich collection of results, some of which are very surprising.
We proved that a countable product of topological spaces with
property P has property P, where P is any of the following:
(i) T0-space
(ii)
T1-space
(iii)
Hausdorï¬€
(iv)
metrizable
(v) connected
(vi) totally disconnected
(vii) second countable.
It
is also true when P is compact, this result being the Tychonoï¬€The-
orem for countable products. The proof of the countable Tychonoï¬€
Theorem for metrizable spaces presented here is quite diï¬€erent from
the standard one which appears in the next section. Our proof relies
on the Cantor Spaces.
The Cantor Space was deï¬ned to be a certain subspace of [0, 1].
Later is was shown that it is homeomorphic to a countably inï¬nite
product of 2-point discrete spaces.
The Cantor Space appears to
be the kind of pathological example pure mathematicians are fond
of producing in order to show that some general statement is false.
But it turns out to be much more than this.
The Alexandroï¬€-Urysohn Theorem says that every compact metriz-
able space is an image of the Cantor Space. In particular [0, 1] and
the Hilbert cube (a countable inï¬nite product of copies of [0, 1]) is a
continuous image of the Cantor Space. This leads us to the existence
of space-ï¬lling curves â€“ in particular, we show that there exists a con-
tinuous map of [0, 1] onto the cube [0, 1]n , for each positive integer n.
We stated, but did not prove, the Hahn-Mazurkiewicz Theorem: The
Hausdorï¬€space (X,Ï„) is an image of [0, 1] if and only if it is compact
connected locally connected and metrizable.
Finally we mention Urysohnâ€™s Theorem, which says that a space
is separable and metrizable if and only if it is homeomorphic to a
subspace of the Hilbert cube.
This shows that [0, 1] is not just a
â€œniceâ€ topological space, but a â€œgeneratorâ€ of the important class
of separable metrizable spaces via the formation of subspaces and
countable products.

In Chapter 9 we deï¬ned the product of a countably inï¬nite family
of topological spaces. We now proceed to deï¬ne the product of any
family of topological spaces by replacing the set {1, 2, . . . , n, . . .} by an
arbitrary index set I. The central result will be the general Tychonoï¬€
Theorem.
189

190
Topology Without Tears

10.2
Zornâ€™s Lemma
Our next task is to prove the general Tychonoï¬€Theorem which says
that any product of compact spaces is compact. However, to do this
we need to use Zornâ€™s Lemma which requires a little preparation.
10.2.1
Deï¬nition.
A partial order on a set X is a binary relation,
denoted by â‰¤, which has the properties:
(i) x â‰¤x, for all x âˆˆX
(reï¬‚exive)
(ii) if x â‰¤y and y â‰¤x then x = y
(antisymmetric), and
(iii) if x â‰¤y and y â‰¤z then x â‰¤z
(transitive)
for x, y and z and X
The set X equipped with the partial order â‰¤is called a partially
ordered set and denoted by (X, â‰¤). IF x â‰¤y and x Ì¸= y we write x < y.
10.2.2
Examples.
The prototype of a partially ordered set is
the set N of all natural numbers equipped with the usual ordering of
natural numbers.
Similarly the sets Z, Q, and R with their usual orderings form
partially ordered sets.
10.2.3
Example.
Let N be the set of natural numbers and â€œ â‰¤â€²â€²
be deï¬ned as follows:
n â‰¤m if n divides m
So 3 â‰¤6 but 3 Ì¸â‰¤5. (It is left as an exercise to verify that with this
ordering N is a partially ordered set.)

194
Topology Without Tears
10.2.4
Example.
Let X be the class of all subsets of a set U. We
can deï¬ne a partial ordering on X by putting
A â‰¤B if A is a subset of B
where A and B are in X.
It is easily veriï¬ed that this is a partial order.
10.2.5
Example.
Let (X, â‰¤) be a partially ordered set. We can
deï¬ne a new partial order â‰¤âˆ—on X by deï¬ning
x â‰¤âˆ—y
if
y â‰¤x.
10.2.6
Example.
There is a convenient way of picturing partially
ordered sets â€“ this is by an order diagram.
An element x is less than an element y if and only if one can go
from x to y by moving upwards on line segments. So in our order

10.2.7
Deï¬nition.
Two elements x and y of a partially ordered
set (X, â‰¤) are said to be comparable if either x â‰¤y or y â‰¤x.
10.2.8
Remark.
We saw in the order diagram above that the
elements d and c are not comparable. Also e and f are not comparable.
In N, Q, R, and Z with the usual orderings every two elements are
comparable.
In Example 10.2.4, 3 and 5 are not comparable.
10.2.9
Deï¬nition.
A partially ordered set (X, â‰¤) is said to be
linearly ordered if every two elements are comparable. The order â‰¤is
then said to a linear order.
10.2.10
Examples.
The usual orders on N, Q, R, and Z are linear
orders.
The partial order of Example 10.2.4 is not a linear order (if U has
at least two points).
10.2.11
Deï¬nition.
Let (X, â‰¤) be a partially ordered set. Then
an element s âˆˆX is said to be the greatest element of X if x â‰¤s, for
all x âˆˆX.
10.2.12
Deï¬nition.
Let (X, â‰¤) be a partially ordered set and Y
a subset of X. An element t âˆˆX is said to be an upper bound for y if
y â‰¤t, for all y âˆˆY .
It is important to note that an upper bound for Y need not be in

10.2.13
Deï¬nition.
Let (X, â‰¤) be a partially ordered set. Then
an

plies w = x.

Topology Without Tears
10.2.14
Remark.
It is important to distinguish between maximal
elements and greatest elements. Consider the order diagram in Remark
10.2.6.
There is no greatest element!
However, j, h, and i are all
maximal elements.
10.2.15
Remark.
We can now state Zornâ€™s Lemma. Despite the
name â€œLemmaâ€, it is, in fact, an axiom and cannot be proved. It is
equivalent to various other axioms of Set Theory such as the Axiom
of Choice and the Well-Ordering Theorem. [See, for example, Paul
R. Halmos, â€œNaive Set Theoryâ€ (Van Nostrand Reinhold Co., 1960)
or R.L. Wilder, â€œIntroduction to the Foundations of Mathematicsâ€
(Wiley, 1952).] We shall take Zornâ€™s Lemma as one of the axioms of
our set theory and so use it whenever we wish.
10.2.16
Axiom.
(Zornâ€™s Lemma)
Let (X, â‰¤) be a non-empty
partially ordered set in which every subset which is linearly ordered
has an upper bound. Then (X, â‰¤) has a maximal element.
10.2.17
Example.
Let us apply Zornâ€™s Lemma to the lattice
diagram of Remark 10.2.6. There are many linearly ordered subsets:
{i, g, b, a}, {g, b, a}, {b, a}, {g, b}, {i, g}, {a}, {b},
{g}, {i}, {i, b, a}, {i, g, a}, {i.a}, {g, a}, {h, g, e},
{h, e}, {g, e}, etc.
Each of these has an upper bound âˆ’âˆ’i, i, i, i, i, i, i, i, i, i, i, i, i, h, h, h,
etc.
Zornâ€™s Lemma then says that there is a maximal element. In fact
there are 3 maximal elements, j, h and i.

10.3.1
Deï¬nition.

Proposition.

Then

Proof.

The converse statement is proved similarly.

Let X be a set and F a family of subsets of X

10.3.6
Proposition.
Let (X, d) be a metric space.

Proof.

10.4 Stone-Ë˜Cech Compactiï¬cation
10.4.1
Deï¬nition.
Let (X,Ï„) be a topological space, (Î²X,Ï„â€²)

WARNING.
The mapping Î² is usually not surjective, so Î²(X) is

10.4.2
Remark.
Those familiar with category theory should im-
mediately recognize that the existence of the Stone-Ë˜Cech compactiï¬ca-
tion follows from the Freyd adjoint functor theorem. [We are seeking
a left adjoint to the forgetful functor from the category of compact
Hausdorï¬€spaces and continuous functions to the category of topo-
logical spaces and continuous functions.] For a discussion of this see

210
Topology Without Tears
S. Maclane, â€œCategories for the working mathematicianâ€ (Graduate
Texts in Mathematics 5, Springer-Verlag, 1971.)
While the Stone-Ë˜Cech compactiï¬cation exists for all topological
spaces, it assumes more signiï¬cance in the case of Tychonoï¬€spaces.
For the mapping Î² is an embedding if and only if the space (X,Ï„)
is Tychonoï¬€. The â€œonly ifâ€ part of this is clear, since the compact
Hausdorï¬€space (Î²X,Ï„â€²) is a Tychonoï¬€space and so, therefore, is any
subspace of it.

10.4.9
Remark.
Note that if (X,Ï„) is any Tychonoï¬€space and
(Î²X,Ï„â€²) together with Î²: (X,Ï„) â†’(Î²X,Ï„â€²) is its Stone-Ë˜Cech com-

10.4.10
Remark.
If (X,Ï„) is any compact Hausdorï¬€space then
the Stone-Ë˜Cech compactiï¬cation of (X,Ï„) is (X,Ï„) itself. Obviously
(X,Ï„) together with the identity mapping into itself has the required
property of a Stone-Ë˜Cech compactiï¬cation. By uniqueness, it is the
Stone-Ë˜Cech compactiï¬cation. This could also be seen from the proof of
Proposition 10.4.5 where we saw that for the compact Hausdorï¬€space
(Y,Ï„â€²â€²) the mapping eY : (Y,Ï„â€²â€²) âˆ’â†’(eY (Y ),Ï„â€²â€²â€²) is a homeomorphism.
10.4.11
Remark.
Stone-Ë˜Cech compactiï¬cations of even quite nice
spaces are usually complicated. For example [0, 1] is not the Stone-
Ë˜Cech compactiï¬cation of (0, 1], since the continuous mapping Ï†: (0, 1] âˆ’â†’
[âˆ’1, 1] given by Ï†(x) = sin(1

Î¦: [0, 1] âˆ’â†’[âˆ’1, 1]. Indeed it can be shown that the Stone-Ë˜Cech com-
pactiï¬cation of (0, 1] is not metrizable.

10.5
Postscript
At long last we deï¬ned the product of an arbitrary number of topo-
logical spaces and proved the general Tychonoï¬€Theorem. We also
extended the Embedding Lemma to the general case. This we used to
characterize the Tychonoï¬€spaces as those which are homeomorphic
to a subspace of a cube (that is, a product of copies of [0, 1]).
Urysohnâ€™s Lemma allowed us to obtain the following relations
between the the separation properties:
T4 â‡’T3 1
2 â‡’T3 â‡’T2 â‡’T1 â‡’T0.
Further, both compact Hausdorï¬€and metrizable imply T4.
We have also seen a serious metrization theorem â€“ namely Urysohnâ€™s
Metrization Theorem, which says that every regular second countable
Hausdorï¬€space is metrizable.
